The Scriptural Proof from Moses' Request
Moses’ request and God’s conditional response are used as a scriptural proof for vision’s possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural proof from Moses' request for the permissibility of seeing Allah, and how the conditional response reinforces the possibility.

Core Idea
Moses’ request and God’s conditional answer prove seeing Allah is possible.

This lesson examines a key scriptural argument used by Sunni theologians to defend the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The proof is drawn from the story of Moses asking to see Allah in Surah al-A‘raf. By analyzing the logic behind Moses’ request and Allah’s response, we see that both the act of asking and the conditional reply imply that vision is rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument
Identify why Moses asking proves possibility: if impossible, he wouldn't ask.
2
Understand the second argument
Examine Allah's conditioning on the mountain's stability; the conditional link shows vision is possible.
3
Consider the objection and conclusion
The text mentions an objection about Moses' intent, but the proof stands on the logic of the request and condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل على الجواز أيضاً بدليل سمعيّ، وهو: أنَّ موسى عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام قد سألها بقوله تعالى: رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرْ إِلَيْكَ فلو لم تكن جائزة ما سألها ، وإلا كان طلبها إما جهلاً بأحكام الألوهية، وإمَّا سفهاً أو عبثاً بطلب المحال، والأنبياء منزهون عن ذلك كله .
وأنَّ الله تعالى قد علقها على ممكن – وهو استقرار الجبل - والمعلَّق على الممكن ممكن، إذ معنى التعليق: الإخبار بوقوع المعلق عند ثبوت المعلق عليه، والمحال لا يقع على شيء من التقادير الممكنة، فلو لم تكن ممكنة لزم الخُلف في خبره تعالى، وهو محال. وما قيل من أَنَّ سؤال موسی عليه السَّلام لم يكن لتحصيل مطلوبه،
And [the author] also argued for permissibility by a scriptural proof, which is: that Moses, peace and blessings be upon him, asked for it by His statement the Exalted: 'My Lord, show me, that I may look at You.' For if it were not permissible, he would not have asked it; otherwise, his request would be either ignorance of the rulings of divinity, or foolishness and futility in asking the impossible—and the prophets are innocent of all that. And that Allah the Exalted conditioned it upon something possible—namely, the stability of the mountain. And that which is conditioned upon a possible is possible, for the meaning of conditioning is informing of the occurrence of the conditioned thing when the condition is realized, and the impossible does not occur under any possible estimation. So if it were not possible, there would be a breach in His report the Exalted—which is impossible. And what is said that Moses' question was not for obtaining his request...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural proof (dalīl sam'ī) for the permissibility of seeing Allah. It is based on the story of Moses (Qur'an 7:143). The proof proceeds in two steps: First, Moses asked to see Allah. If vision were impossible, he would not have asked, because that would imply either ignorance of God's nature, or foolishness and frivolity—all of which are impossible for prophets. Therefore, vision must be possible. Second, Allah responded by conditioning the vision upon the mountain's stability, which is a possible event. The conditional clause ('if the mountain remains in its place, then you shall see Me') shows that the conditioned thing (the vision) is possible, because it is linked to a possible condition. If vision were impossible, Allah's statement would be false, which is impossible. The passage ends by mentioning an objection—that Moses' request was not for actual attainment—but this objection is not answered here.

Key Terms
دليل سمعي
سؤال موسى
الاستقرار الجبل
تعليق الممكن
رب أرني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' request was just a test or not meant to be taken literally, so it doesn't prove vision is possible.
Correction: The proof argues that prophets do not ask the impossible; the very act of asking shows it is possible. Additionally, Allah's conditional response would be meaningless if vision were impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument from the story of Moses that the author uses to prove vision is possible?
A) Moses would not ask the impossible
B) Allah conditioned the vision on a possible event
C) The mountain actually did not move
D) The prophets are infallible

Key Takeaway
The scriptural proof from Moses shows that the possibility of seeing Allah is established both by the fact that a prophet asked for it and by Allah’s conditional response linking it to a possible event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Moses, a great prophet, dared to ask to see Allah. What 'impossible' longing have you suppressed in your heart for fear that it is too much to ask from Allah? Will you bring it to Him this week with the same boldness?
?
If you truly believe that seeing Allah is possible, how would that change the way you yearn for Him in your prayers? What would you do differently today to cultivate that desire?
?
Allah conditioned the vision on a miracle. In what area of your life have you given up hope because you see it as impossible, forgetting that the One who made the mountain stable can make any condition possible for you?

Introduction to Samiyyat: Revelation-Based Theology
Section heading announcing the third major division: Samiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'al-samiyyat' and its role as a distinct category in Islamic theological works.

Core Idea
Samiyyat are doctrines known through revelation, not pure reason.

This brief section heading marks a major turning point in the theological text. After discussing matters accessible to reason (ilahiyyat), the author now turns to doctrines that require revealed texts. Understanding this term will help you navigate the structure of classical kalam works.

Guided Path
1
Read the heading carefully
Read the Arabic heading and its translation. Notice its brevity—it functions as a clear structural marker.
The heading is only two lines, but it carries significant meaning.
2
Understand the term 'al-samiyyat'
Reflect on the derivation from 'sam'' (hearing/revelation). These are matters that cannot be fully known by reason alone.
Contrast with 'aqliyyat' (rational matters).
3
Connect to the work's structure
Consider that the text has three main sections: (1) ilahiyyat, (2) possibly nubuwwat or analogous, and (3) samiyyat. This heading introduces the final section.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why would a theologian need a separate section for revealed doctrines? What topics might be included?
Common topics include prophethood, miracles, the afterlife, and eschatology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث
السمعيات
The third section: al-samiyyat (matters known through revelation).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a section heading. The author marks the beginning of the third major division of the work, which he titles 'al-Samiyyat'. In Islamic theology, 'samiyyat' refers to doctrines that are established primarily through revealed texts (sam') rather than through pure rational demonstration. This includes topics such as prophethood, miracles, the afterlife, and other matters that require scriptural evidence. The heading signals a shift from the earlier sections—likely on divine existence and attributes (ilahiyyat) and perhaps on rational judgments—to matters that depend on transmitted knowledge.

Key Terms
السمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think samiyyat are irrational or arbitrary.
Correction: Rather, they are doctrines that reason cannot fully reach without revelation, but they are not contrary to reason.

Checkpoint
What does the term 'al-samiyyat' refer to in this context?
A) Matters known through sensory perception
B) Matters known through revelation
C) Matters known through logical reasoning
D) Matters related to divine attributes

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-samiyyat' designates theological doctrines that depend on revelation (sam') for their establishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Name one belief you hold that you would never have known without revelation. How would your life be different if you didn't have that knowledge?
?
Are there any Islamic teachings that you struggle to accept because they seem to contradict your reason? How does the concept of samiyyat challenge you to submit to revelation even when you don't fully understand?

The Obligation of Believing in the Reckoning
Defines al-hisab, states obligation, provides scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the Reckoning (al-hisab), its obligatory nature, the proof from the three sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma), and that denial constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Belief in the Reckoning is an obligatory article of faith proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

The author now turns from divine matters and prophethoods to the sam‘iyyat (things heard). The first topic is belief in the Reckoning (al-hisab). This passage establishes that affirming the Reckoning is a necessary duty, and denial is kufr, based on scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three-part structure: definition, obligation, and evidence.
2
Identify the key definitions
The passage gives both a linguistic and a technical definition of 'al-hisab'. Make sure you understand the difference.
Linguistic meaning is 'counting'; technical meaning is 'God bringing servants to account in the gathering-place'.
3
List the three proofs
The author provides evidence from the Quran, the Sunnah, and consensus. Identify each proof and note how it is presented.
For Quran: 'Swift in reckoning'. For Sunnah: the hadith 'Take account of yourselves...'. For Ijma: the consensus of Muslims.
4
Understand the ruling
The passage explicitly states that belief in the Reckoning is obligatory and that denial is kufr. Reflect on why this ruling is so firm.
The ruling is based on the established proofs from all three sources.
5
Note the inclusion of the jinn
The end of the passage extends the obligation to the jinn. Consider why this is mentioned and what it implies.
The jinn are also mukallaf, so they are included in the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت مباحث هذا الفنّ ثلاثة : إلهيَّات ونبوات وسمعيات، وقد تقدم الكلام على بيان الأَوَّلين؛ شرع في الثالث وهو السمعيات فقال : (ويلزم) أي : يجب على المكلفين (الإيمان) أي: التصديق (بالحساب). وهو لغة: العد. واصطلاحاً : توقيف الله عباده في المحشر على أعمالهم، فعلاً أو قوله: (أي: يجب على المكلَّفين أي: وجوب الأصول من أنكره كفر لثبوته كتاباً، وسنة وإجماعاً. فالكتاب : قال تعالى : (سَرِيعُ الْحِسَابِ)، وغير ذلك من الآيات. والسُّنَّة قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: «حَاسِبُوا أَنفُسَكُم قبل أن تُحَاسَبوا»، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث. وأجمع المسلمون عليه والمراد بالمكلَّفين ما يشمل الجنَّ؛ لأنَّ لهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا . قوله : ( في المحشر) : بفتح السين وكسرها .
And since the topics of this art are three: divine matters, prophethoods, and things heard (sam‘iyyat), and the discussion on the exposition of the first two has preceded, he began on the third, which is the things heard (sam‘iyyat), and said: (And it is necessary) i.e., obligatory upon the obligated believers (al-mukallafūn) (the belief) i.e., affirmation (in the Reckoning). And it is linguistically: counting. And terminologically: God's bringing His servants to account in the gathering-place for their deeds, actual or spoken. (i.e., it is obligatory upon the obligated believers, i.e., an obligatory matter of the principles; whoever denies it disbelieves because it is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. So the Book: He said, Exalted: 'Swift in reckoning' and other verses. And the Sunnah: he said, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Take account of yourselves before you are taken to account' and other hadiths. And the Muslims have agreed upon it. And the intended meaning of 'the obligated believers' includes the jinn, because they have what we have and are liable for what we are liable for. His statement: (in the gathering-place): with the sin (sin) with a fatha [or] with a kasra).
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage defines the belief in the Reckoning (al-hisab) as part of the sam‘iyyat (things heard), the third major division of kalam after ilahiyyat and nubuwwat. The author states that it is obligatory (lazim/wajib) upon the mukallafun (those legally responsible) to affirm it. He gives the linguistic meaning ('counting') and the technical meaning: God making His servants stand account on the Day of Gathering for their actions and words. He then emphasizes that denial constitutes disbelief (kufr) because the Reckoning is proven by three sources: Quran (e.g., 'Swift in reckoning'), Sunnah (the hadith 'Take account of yourselves...'), and consensus (ijma‘). Finally, he extends the obligation to the jinn as well, noting they share the same duties and responsibilities as humans. The comment on the word 'al-mahshar' notes two variant vowellings.

المكلَّفون
الإيمان
السمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think belief in the Reckoning is merely recommended, not obligatory.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is an obligatory matter of principles; denial is kufr because it is proven by Book, Sunnah, and consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is denying the Reckoning considered disbelief (kufr)?
A) Because it is established by the Quran alone.
B) Because the Reckoning is rationally impossible.
C) Because it is established by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus.
D) Because the jinn are not included.

Key Takeaway
Belief in the Reckoning is a foundational obligation of faith, proven by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, and its denial constitutes disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every deed—every word and action—will be brought to account? How would your daily choices change if you lived with that certainty today, not just as an abstract belief?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Take account of yourselves before you are taken to account.' When was the last time you sincerely held yourself accountable for a sin? What stops you from doing it daily?
?
The obligation of belief in the Reckoning applies even to the jinn. If you are human, are you taking this obligation seriously enough to prepare for that day? What specific step will you take this week to remind yourself of the reckoning?

The Obligation of Belief in al-Hisab
Sanusi states the obligation of belief in al-Hisab and its status as a foundational principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in al-Hisab is an obligatory article of faith, denial of which constitutes disbelief, and that it belongs to the category of sam'iyyat.

Core Idea
Belief in the Reckoning is obligatory; denial is kufr.

The author introduces the third branch of kalam—sam'iyyat—and within it, the obligation of belief in al-Hisab. Before exploring its definition and proofs, he establishes that this belief is a fundamental duty and that rejecting it places one outside the fold of Islam. This lesson grounds you in the basic ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sam'iyyat
The passage begins by noting that kalam has three branches: ilahiyyat, nubuwwat, and sam'iyyat. Recognize that we are now entering the third.
This is a standard structure in kalam works.
2
Identify the obligation statement
The matn says 'ويلزم الإيمان بالحساب'. Understand that 'yelzam' indicates a necessary duty upon every mukallaf.
The commentary specifies this is wajib as a principle.
3
Note the consequence of denial
The text states that whoever denies al-Hisab becomes a disbeliever (kafir) because it is established by definitive evidence.
This shows the gravity of this belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالحساب
ولما كانت مباحث هذا الفنّ ثلاثة : إلهيَّات ونبوات وسمعيات، وقد تقدم الكلام على بيان الأَوَّلين؛ شرع في الثالث وهو السمعيات فقال :
(ويلزم) أي : يجب على المكلفين (الإيمان) أي: التصديق (بالحساب).
وهو لغة: العد.
واصطلاحاً : توقيف الله عباده في المحشر على أعمالهم، فعلاً أو قولاً أو اعتقاداً.
قوله: (أي: يجب على المكلَّفين) أي: وجوب الأصول من أنكره كفر لثبوته كتاباً، وسنة وإجماعاً.
فالكتاب : قال تعالى : (سَرِيعُ الْحِسَابِ)، وغير ذلك من الآيات. والسُّنَّة قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: «حَاسِبُوا أَنفُسَكُم قبل أن تُحَاسَبوا»، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث.
وأجمع المسلمون عليه والمراد بالمكلَّفين ما يشمل الجنَّ؛ لأنَّ لهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا .
قوله : ( في المحشر) : بفتح السين وكسرها .
تفصيلاً بأن يكلّمهم الله تعالى بكلام قديم ليس بحرف ولا صوت بأن يُزيل عنهم الحجاب حتّى يسمعوه، أو بصوت يخلقه الله تعالى يدل عليه وقد يكون من الملائكة فقط، وقد يكون منه تعالى ومن الملائكة جميعاً .
وكيفيته مختلفة فمنه اليسير ومنه العسير، والسِّرُّ والجهر، والفضل والعدل، على حسب الأعمال، فيغفر لمن يشاء ويعذِّب من يشاء. ويكون للمؤمنين والكافرين إنساً وجنّاً، بعد أخذهم الكُتُب لقوله تعالى: فَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابَهُ بِيَمِينِهِ، فَسَوْفَ يُحَاسَبُ حِسَابًا يَسِيرًا وَيَنقَلِبُ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ مَسْرُورًا .
وأيسر الحساب محاسبة الله فقط حتّى لا يعلم بذلك إنس ولا جن ولا مَلَك، يقول تعالى له هذه سيئاتك قد غفرتها لك، وهذه حسناتك قد ضاعفتها لك .
قوله : (وقد يكون من الملائكة فقط) : أي : وهو أصعبها
قوله : (بعد أخذهم الكتب) : أي : وبعد الشفاعة في فصل القضاء.
قوله : (وأيسر الحساب محاسبة الله فقط) : أي : لأنَّ الغالب فيها العفو.
قوله : (يقول تعالى له : هذه سيئاتك ... إلخ ) : أي : بعد أن يضع كنفه عليه وهذا لمن يحبُّ السَّتر على عباد الله .
Belief in the Reckoning. And since the topics of this art are three: divine matters, prophethoods, and transmitted matters (sam'iyyat), and the discussion of the first two has preceded, he began the third which is the transmitted matters and said: (And it is necessary) i.e., obligatory upon the legally responsible (belief) i.e., affirmation (in the Reckoning). It is linguistically: counting. And terminologically: God's stopping His servants in the gathering-place (al-mahshar) for their deeds, whether action, speech, or belief, in detail by God Most High speaking to them with an eternal speech that is not a letter nor a sound by removing the veil from them until they hear it, or with a sound that God creates indicating it, and it may be from the angels only, and it may be from Him Most High and from the angels together. Its modality differs: some is easy and some is difficult, secret and public, favor and justice, according to deeds. He forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills. It is for the believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, after they have taken the books, according to His saying: 'Then as for he who is given his book in his right hand, he will be reckoned with an easy reckoning and return to his family joyful.' (84:7-8) And the easiest reckoning is God's accounting alone such that no human, jinn, nor angel knows of it. He says to him: 'These are your sins, I have forgiven them for you, and these are your good deeds, I have multiplied them for you.' His statement: (and it may be from the angels only) meaning: and it is the most difficult of them. His statement: (after they have taken the books) meaning: and after intercession in the adjudication of judgment. His statement: (And the easiest reckoning is God's accounting alone) meaning: because pardoning predominates in it. His statement: (He says to him: 'These are your sins... etc.') meaning: after He places His protection upon him, and this is for the one who loves concealment of God's servants.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a composite lesson: it introduces the third branch of kalam (sam'iyyat), states the obligation of belief in al-Hisab (the Reckoning), defines it both linguistically and terminologically, provides proofs from Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma', and then explains the modalities of the Reckoning in detail, including glosses on specific phrases. The author begins by noting that the topics of kalam are three: ilahiyyat (divine matters), nubuwwat (prophethoods), and sam'iyyat (transmitted matters). Having covered the first two, he now turns to the third, which includes belief in the Reckoning. The matn (main text) states: 'And it is necessary upon the legally responsible to believe in the Reckoning.' The commentator explains that this is obligatory (wajib) as a matter of fundamental principles (usul); whoever denies it becomes a disbeliever because it is established by the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma'. Then he gives the linguistic definition of 'hisab' as 'counting' (al-'add). The technical (istilahi) definition: 'God's stopping His servants in the gathering-place (al-mahshar) for their deeds, whether action, speech, or belief, in detail.' This 'in detail' is then unpacked: God speaks to them with an eternal speech (kalam qadim) that is not a letter nor a sound, by removing the veil so they hear it directly, or with a created sound that indicates it. This speech may come from the angels alone, or from God and the angels together. The modality (kayfiyya) of the Reckoning varies: some is easy, some difficult; some secret, some public; some with favor, some with justice—all according to deeds. God forgives or punishes as He wills. The Reckoning applies to both believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, after they receive their books (records of deeds). The proof is the Qur'anic verse about the easy reckoning for the righteous (Surah al-Inshiqaq). The author then describes the easiest reckoning: God alone accounts for the person, hidden from all creation, saying that He has forgiven their sins and multiplied their good deeds. Finally, glosses are provided: the reckoning by angels alone is the most difficult; the reckoning occurs after the intercession and adjudication; the easiest reckoning is characterized by predominance of pardon; and the hidden accounting is for those whom God wishes to conceal. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that belief in al-Hisab is a fundamental article of faith, denial of which leads to disbelief, and that its reality is both transcendent (eternal speech) and concrete (varying conditions for different people).

Key Terms
الحساب
السمعيات
الإيمان
المكلَّفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on denying belief in al-Hisab?
A) It is a major sin but not disbelief
B) It is disbelief (kufr)
C) It is permissible if done out of ignorance
D) It is a minor sin

Key Takeaway
Belief in al-Hisab is a foundational obligation; denying it removes one from Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trivialized the thought of standing before Allah for the Reckoning? What change in your daily life would true belief in al-Hisab demand right now?
?
If you truly believed that every action will be accounted for, what is one habit you would immediately abandon and one you would cultivate starting today?
?
How does your current level of preparation for the Reckoning compare to your concern for worldly accountability? What concrete step will you take today to bridge that gap?

Definition and Proofs of al-Hisab
Sanusi defines al-Hisab and provides its proofs from the primary sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of al-Hisab and the three proofs that establish it: Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma'.

Core Idea
Al-Hisab is God reckoning His servants; it is proven by three sources.

Having established the obligation, the author now defines al-Hisab both linguistically and as a theological term. He then provides the evidence: verses from the Qur'an, hadith, and the consensus of the scholars. This lesson gives you the foundational definitions and their scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Learn the linguistic definition
The word 'hisab' in Arabic means counting. The commentary says 'هو لغة: العد'.
This is the root meaning.
2
Learn the technical definition
Terminologically, it is God stopping His servants in the gathering place to account for their actions, speech, and beliefs.
Note the three categories: actions, speech, beliefs.
3
Review the proofs
The author cites the Book (Qur'an verse 'سَرِيعُ الْحِسَابِ'), the Sunnah (hadith 'حَاسِبُوا أَنْفُسَكُمْ'), and Ijma' (consensus).
These are the three standard sources of evidence.

?

Modalities of the Reckoning
Sanusi elaborates on the modalities of the Reckoning, including types of speech and the easiest form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different ways the Reckoning takes place: through eternal speech or created sound, from angels or from God, and the easiest form of hidden reckoning.

Core Idea
The Reckoning varies: easy or hard, secret or public, according to deeds.

After establishing the definition and proof, the author explains how the Reckoning occurs. He describes that God may speak directly with eternal speech or through created sound, that angels may be involved, and that the experience differs based on one's deeds. The easiest form is a secret reckoning between God and the servant, with full forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the modes of divine speech
The reckoning involves God speaking to people, either with eternal speech (not letters/sounds) by removing the veil, or with a created sound.
This preserves God's transcendence.
2
Distinguish who speaks
Sometimes only angels speak, sometimes God alone, sometimes both. The angels-only reckoning is the most difficult.
Note the variation.
3
Learn about the easiest reckoning
The easiest is when God alone accounts for the person, hidden from all creation, forgiving sins and multiplying good deeds.
This is for those whom God wishes to conceal.

الإيمان بالحساب
ولما كانت مباحث هذا الفنّ ثلاثة : إلهيَّات ونبوات وسمعيات، وقد تقدم الكلام على بيان الأَوَّلين؛ شرع في الثالث وهو السمعيات فقال :
(ويلزم) أي : يجب على المكلفين (الإيمان) أي: التصديق (بالحساب).
وهو لغة: العد.
واصطلاحاً : توقيف الله عباده في المحشر على أعمالهم، فعلاً أو قولاً أو اعتقاداً.
قوله: (أي: يجب على المكلَّفين) أي: وجوب الأصول من أنكره كفر لثبوته كتاباً، وسنة وإجماعاً.
فالكتاب : قال تعالى : (سَرِيعُ الْحِسَابِ)، وغير ذلك من الآيات. والسُّنَّة قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: «حَاسِبُوا أَنفُسَكُم قبل أن تُحَاسَبوا»، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث.
وأجمع المسلمون عليه والمراد بالمكلَّفين ما يشمل الجنَّ؛ لأنَّ لهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا .
قوله : ( في المحشر) : بفتح السين وكسرها .
تفصيلاً بأن يكلّمهم الله تعالى بكلام قديم ليس بحرف ولا صوت بأن يُزيل عنهم الحجاب حتّى يسمعوه، أو بصوت يخلقه الله تعالى يدل عليه وقد يكون من الملائكة فقط، وقد يكون منه تعالى ومن الملائكة جميعاً .
وكيفيته مختلفة فمنه اليسير ومنه العسير، والسِّرُّ والجهر، والفضل والعدل، على حسب الأعمال، فيغفر لمن يشاء ويعذِّب من يشاء. ويكون للمؤمنين والكافرين إنساً وجنّاً، بعد أخذهم الكُتُب لقوله تعالى: فَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابَهُ بِيَمِينِهِ، فَسَوْفَ يُحَاسَبُ حِسَابًا يَسِيرًا وَيَنقَلِبُ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ مَسْرُورًا .
وأيسر الحساب محاسبة الله فقط حتّى لا يعلم بذلك إنس ولا جن ولا مَلَك، يقول تعالى له هذه سيئاتك قد غفرتها لك، وهذه حسناتك قد ضاعفتها لك .
قوله : (وقد يكون من الملائكة فقط) : أي : وهو أصعبها
قوله : (بعد أخذهم الكتب) : أي : وبعد الشفاعة في فصل القضاء.
قوله : (وأيسر الحساب محاسبة الله فقط) : أي : لأنَّ الغالب فيها العفو.
قوله : (يقول تعالى له : هذه سيئاتك ... إلخ ) : أي : بعد أن يضع كنفه عليه وهذا لمن يحبُّ السَّتر على عباد الله .
Belief in the Reckoning. And since the topics of this art are three: divine matters, prophethoods, and transmitted matters (sam'iyyat), and the discussion of the first two has preceded, he began the third which is the transmitted matters and said: (And it is necessary) i.e., obligatory upon the legally responsible (belief) i.e., affirmation (in the Reckoning). It is linguistically: counting. And terminologically: God's stopping His servants in the gathering-place (al-mahshar) for their deeds, whether action, speech, or belief, in detail by God Most High speaking to them with an eternal speech that is not a letter nor a sound by removing the veil from them until they hear it, or with a sound that God creates indicating it, and it may be from the angels only, and it may be from Him Most High and from the angels together. Its modality differs: some is easy and some is difficult, secret and public, favor and justice, according to deeds. He forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills. It is for the believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, after they have taken the books, according to His saying: 'Then as for he who is given his book in his right hand, he will be reckoned with an easy reckoning and return to his family joyful.' (84:7-8) And the easiest reckoning is God's accounting alone such that no human, jinn, nor angel knows of it. He says to him: 'These are your sins, I have forgiven them for you, and these are your good deeds, I have multiplied them for you.' His statement: (and it may be from the angels only) meaning: and it is the most difficult of them. His statement: (after they have taken the books) meaning: and after intercession in the adjudication of judgment. His statement: (And the easiest reckoning is God's accounting alone) meaning: because pardoning predominates in it. His statement: (He says to him: 'These are your sins... etc.') meaning: after He places His protection upon him, and this is for the one who loves concealment of God's servants.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a composite lesson: it introduces the third branch of kalam (sam'iyyat), states the obligation of belief in al-Hisab (the Reckoning), defines it both linguistically and terminologically, provides proofs from Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma', and then explains the modalities of the Reckoning in detail, including glosses on specific phrases. The author begins by noting that the topics of kalam are three: ilahiyyat (divine matters), nubuwwat (prophethoods), and sam'iyyat (transmitted matters). Having covered the first two, he now turns to the third, which includes belief in the Reckoning. The matn (main text) states: 'And it is necessary upon the legally responsible to believe in the Reckoning.' The commentator explains that this is obligatory (wajib) as a matter of fundamental principles (usul); whoever denies it becomes a disbeliever because it is established by the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma'. Then he gives the linguistic definition of 'hisab' as 'counting' (al-'add). The technical (istilahi) definition: 'God's stopping His servants in the gathering-place (al-mahshar) for their deeds, whether action, speech, or belief, in detail.' This 'in detail' is then unpacked: God speaks to them with an eternal speech (kalam qadim) that is not a letter nor a sound, by removing the veil so they hear it directly, or with a created sound that indicates it. This speech may come from the angels alone, or from God and the angels together. The modality (kayfiyya) of the Reckoning varies: some is easy, some difficult; some secret, some public; some with favor, some with justice—all according to deeds. God forgives or punishes as He wills. The Reckoning applies to both believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, after they receive their books (records of deeds). The proof is the Qur'anic verse about the easy reckoning for the righteous (Surah al-Inshiqaq). The author then describes the easiest reckoning: God alone accounts for the person, hidden from all creation, saying that He has forgiven their sins and multiplied their good deeds. Finally, glosses are provided: the reckoning by angels alone is the most difficult; the reckoning occurs after the intercession and adjudication; the easiest reckoning is characterized by predominance of pardon; and the hidden accounting is for those whom God wishes to conceal. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that belief in al-Hisab is a fundamental article of faith, denial of which leads to disbelief, and that its reality is both transcendent (eternal speech) and concrete (varying conditions for different people).

المحشر
الشفاعة
الكتب
الحساب اليسير

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The easiest reckoning involves God saying to you privately: 'These are your sins, I have forgiven them; these are your good deeds, I have multiplied them.' Are you living as if that is your hope—or are you relying on your own merits and forgetting that even your good deeds are multiplied only by His grace?
?
The text says the reckoning varies according to deeds. If you knew that your secret sins and public deeds are being weighed, and that the hardest reckoning comes from angels alone, what fear or hope does that stir in you right now? How will you change your hidden life today?

The Divine Accounting: Modes, Ease, and the Exceptional Seventy Thousand
Sanusi explains the nature of divine accounting and the exception of seventy thousand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various modes of divine accounting on the Day of Judgment, including the easiest form of hidden reckoning and the exceptional case of seventy thousand believers.

Core Idea
God's accounting fits each deed—some easy, some hard; the easiest is hidden from all creation.

This passage from Sanusi's creed presents a detailed picture of the Last Day's accounting. It describes how God addresses people—sometimes directly with eternal speech, sometimes through angels—and how the reckoning varies in difficulty, secrecy, and outcome. The author also highlights the easiest accounting, where God privately forgives and multiplies good deeds, and notes the special exception of seventy thousand saints, led by Abu Bakr, who enter Paradise without account. As we read, notice how these details reflect divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the types of divine speech
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two kinds of divine speech: eternal speech without letters/sounds, and created sounds. Also note who delivers the speech: God alone, angels alone, or both.
This sets the foundation for understanding the varieties of accounting.
2
Contrast the easiest and hardest accounting
The passage says accounting is sometimes easy, sometimes hard, secret or public, favor or justice. The hardest involves only angels; the easiest is when God alone reckons privately, hiding it from all creatures.
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it shows divine mercy.
3
Understand the exception of seventy thousand
After describing general accounting, Sanusi notes that infallible prophets are not accounted harshly, and seventy thousand, with Abu Bakr as their leader, enter Paradise without any accounting. This is a special grace.
This exception underscores God's generosity and the high rank of Abu Bakr.

(۱) سورة الانشقاق : (۹۷). ولا يكون للمعصومين، ويستثنى ممَّن يحاسب سبعون ألفاً، أفضلُهم أبو بكر الصديق الله ، فإنّهم يدخلون الجنة بغير حساب كما ورد بذلك الحديث. وهذه الأمّة وإن كانت آخر الأمم إلا أنّها تُقدَّم في الآخرة في الحساب وغيره .
In speech or belief, in detail: that God addresses them with an eternal speech that is not a letter or a sound by removing the veil from them so they hear it, or with a sound that God creates indicating it; and it may be from the angels alone, or from God and the angels together. Its manner differs: some is easy, some hard; secret and public; favor and justice, according to deeds. He forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills. It occurs for believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, after they take their books, as God says: 'Then as for he who is given his book in his right hand, he will be judged an easy judgment and return to his family joyful' (Quran 84:7-9). And the easiest of accounting is only God's accounting, such that no human, jinn, or angel knows it. He says to him: 'These are your sins, I have forgiven them for you; these are your good deeds, I have multiplied them for you.' Forgiveness. His words: 'and it may be from the angels alone' means: it is the hardest. His words: 'after they take the books' means: after intercession in the decision of judgment. His words: 'and the easiest of accounting is only God's accounting' means: because the predominant is... And this ummah, though it is the last of nations, will be advanced in the Hereafter in the accounting and other things.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the nature of divine accounting (hisab) on the Day of Judgment. It begins by stating that God addresses people either with eternal speech or created sounds, sometimes directly, sometimes through angels. The accounting varies in ease and hardship, secrecy and openness, according to one's deeds. It applies to all humans and jinn after they receive their books. The easiest accounting is when God hides it from all creation, forgiving and multiplying good deeds. The commentator then clarifies specific phrases: the hardest type of accounting involves angels only; the context of 'after taking books' occurs after intercession. The phrase 'easiest accounting' refers to God's private reckoning. The passage also notes that the infallible (prophets) are exempt from harsh accounting, and seventy thousand, led by Abu Bakr, enter Paradise without any account, as per hadith. The ummah, though last, is given precedence in the Hereafter.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The easiest accounting is hidden from all creatures and involves only God's private forgiveness and multiplication of good deeds.

?

?

Belief in the Gathering (al-Hashr) and the Order of Rising
Definition of al-Hashr and order of rising with a hadith illustration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic belief in the gathering of bodies after resurrection and the specific order in which people rise from their graves, as well as the significance of the seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account.

Core Idea
The gathering of bodies is a real event with a divinely ordained order of resurrection.

This lesson presents the definition of al-Hashr—the gathering of resurrected bodies—and explains the order of rising from the graves as derived from prophetic traditions. It also touches upon a famous hadith about seventy thousand believers who enter Paradise without reckoning, clarifying their special status. The passage ties together eschatological beliefs with concrete details that strengthen faith in the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview. Notice the definition of al-Hashr as driving bodies to the standing-place, and the mention of a hadith about seventy thousand.
The hadith is a parenthetical illustration, not the main definition.
2
Identify the order of rising
The passage lists who rises first: the Prophet Muhammad (al-Mustafa), then his two companions (Abu Bakr and Umar), then the people of al-Baqi', then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then the rest. Identify each group in the Arabic.
This order reflects the status and proximity to the Prophet.
3
Understand the seventy thousand hadith
The hadith speaks of seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account, and some narrations add that each brings a thousand more. These are called 'the freed ones of the Merciful'. Note how this hadith is used to illustrate God's generosity.
This is a well-known hadith; do not confuse it with the main topic.
4
Reflect on the relationship between the two parts
Consider why the author included the hadith within the discussion of al-Hashr. It may serve as a comfort or motivation, showing that the gathering is not only about judgment but also about mercy for the righteous.
The hadith is a parenthetical insert.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you remember the first to rise from the graves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالحشر (و) يجب الإيمان بـ (الحشر) أي: حشر الأجساد، وهو: سَوْقُها إلى الموقف المسمّى بالحشر بعد بعثهم من قبورهم قوله : (كما ورد بذلك الحديث وهو ما معناه : أعطاني ربي سبعون ألفاً يدخلون الجنَّة بغير حساب، فاستزدت ربي فزادني، فقال لي: هكذا وهكذا» (١) ، كناية عن كونه أعطاه من غير عدد، فهؤلاء يسمون عتقاء الرَّحمن، وورد في بعض الرّوايات : إنَّ مع كلّ واحدٍ من السبعين ألفاً ألفاً » (٢) سبعين قوله: (وهو سوقها إلى الموقف أي وأوَّل من تنشقُ عنه الأرض : : : المصطفى ، ثمَّ ،صاحباه ، ثمَّ أهل البقيع ، ثمَّ أهل مكة، ثمّ أهل الشّام، ثمَّ من بقي. (1) أخرجه البخاري (٥٧٥٢) ، ومسلم (٣٧٤/۲۲۰) عن ابن عباس نا، والإمام أحمد في مسنده» (٦/١) ، عن أبي بكر الصديق الله . (۲) أخرجه الإمام أحمد في مسنده (٦/١) وأبو يعلى في مسنده» (۱۱۲) عن أبي بكر الصديق له
Belief in the gathering (al-Hashr). (And) it is obligatory to believe in al-Hashr, i.e., the gathering of bodies, which is: driving them to the standing-place called al-Hashr after their resurrection from their graves. His words: 'As the hadith indicates, and its meaning is: My Lord gave me seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account, so I asked my Lord for more, and He increased me, and said to me: such and such' (1), as a metaphor for giving without number. These are called the freed ones of the Merciful. And it is reported in some narrations that with each of the seventy thousand there are a thousand (2) seventy. His words: 'and it is driving them to the standing-place' means: the first from whom the earth splits open is al-Mustafa (the Chosen), then his two companions, then the people of al-Baqi', then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then the rest. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari (5752), Muslim (374/220) from Ibn Abbas (may God be pleased with him), and Imam Ahmad in his Musnad (6/1) from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may God be pleased with him). (2) Narrated by Imam Ahmad in his Musnad (6/1) and Abu Ya'la in his Musnad (112) from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may God be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage covers the second major article: belief in al-Hashr (the gathering). It defines Hashr as the driving of bodies to the standing-place after resurrection. The commentator then clarifies a hadith about seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account; this hadith is cited as a metaphor for God's generosity, and those seventy thousand are called 'freed ones of the Merciful'. A variant narration adds that each of the seventy thousand has a thousand companions. The commentator then explains the phrase 'driving to the standing-place' by listing the order of those who rise first from the graves: the Prophet Muhammad, then his two companions (Abu Bakr and Umar), then the people of al-Baqi' cemetery, then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then the rest. The passage concludes with hadith citations for the seventy thousand narration.

Key Terms
الحشر
الموقف
عتقاء الرحمن
المصطفى
صاحباه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the seventy thousand are the only ones saved and that others are not saved.
Correction: The seventy thousand are a special group who enter Paradise without account, but many others will also enter Paradise through God's mercy or after judgment. The hadith does not negate salvation for others.

Checkpoint

A) The people of Mecca
B) The people of Syria
C) The Prophet Muhammad and his two companions
D) The people of al-Baqi'

Key Takeaway
Belief in al-Hashr includes accepting both the gathering of bodies and the divinely ordered hierarchy of resurrection, starting with the Prophet and his companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The source teaches that the first to rise from the grave is the Prophet Muhammad, then his companions, then the righteous of different lands. When you picture that day, do you genuinely hope to be among those who rise with the honored groups? What are you doing now to secure that hope?
?
Seventy thousand enter Paradise without account — solely by Allah's mercy. Does this reality shake your reliance on your own good deeds? How will you today cultivate a deeper trust in Allah's mercy rather than your own efforts?
?
If you were to be resurrected tomorrow, would the order of your rising reflect the love you have for the Prophet and his companions? What one change can you make this week to increase that love?

Definition of al-Hashr and the Order of Emergence
Definition and elaboration on al-Hashr with hadith and order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Hashr, its significance in Islamic creed, and the detailed description of the gathering including the hadith about the seventy thousand and the order of resurrection.

Core Idea
Al-Hashr is the driving of bodies to the station after resurrection.

Al-Sawi begins by establishing the reality of al-Hashr as a core belief. He defines it as the gathering of bodies driven to the station after their resurrection from graves, and then elaborates on the hadith about the seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account, calling them 'the freed ones of ar-Rahman'. He also details the order in which people will emerge from their graves, starting with the Prophet ﷺ and his companions, and describes the varying ranks and forms of people during the gathering.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-Hashr
Read the opening sentence of the Arabic passage carefully. It states that believing in al-Hashr is obligatory and gives its meaning as 'driving the bodies to the station called al-Hashr after their resurrection.'
Note that al-Hashr is a physical gathering after the resurrection.
2
Examine the hadith about seventy thousand
The passage then comments on a hadith about seventy thousand entering Paradise without account. Notice that the Prophet ﷺ asked for more and received an unspecified increase, and that these people are called 'the freed ones of ar-Rahman'. There is also a narration that each of the seventy thousand will have a thousand with them.
This hadith highlights Allah's generosity and the special status of those who enter without account.
3
Study the order of emergence from graves
The passage states the order: first the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, then his two companions (Abu Bakr and 'Umar), then the people of al-Baqi', then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then the rest. This shows a hierarchy of honor in resurrection.
This order is based on narrations about the events of the Day of Judgment.
4
Reflect on the varying states of people
After the order, the passage describes that people in the gathering will be in different forms: riders, walkers, and those crawling on their faces, depending on their deeds. This illustrates that the resurrection manifests one's spiritual state.
The forms are not arbitrary but correspond to one's actions in the world.

And it is obligatory to believe in al-Hashr, meaning the gathering of bodies, and it is: driving them to the station called al-Hashr after their resurrection from their graves. His saying: 'As is mentioned in the hadith, the meaning of which is: My Lord gave me seventy thousand who will enter Paradise without account, so I asked my Lord for more, and He increased me, and He said to me: “Thus and thus” (1), as a metaphor for His giving him without number, so these are called the freed ones of ar-Rahman, and it is mentioned in some narrations that with every one of the seventy thousand there are a thousand (2) seventy [thousand?]. His saying: 'And it is driving them to the station' meaning: and the first from whom the earth will split is al-Mustafa, then his two companions, then the people of al-Baqi', then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then whoever remains. And the ranks of people in al-Hashr vary: among them are riders, among them are walkers on their feet, and among them are those who walk on their faces (1), and it will be in different forms according to deeds; among them: whoever is... And the types of al-Hashr are four: two in this world: first: his expulsion, peace and blessings be upon him, of the Jews from Medina to Syria. Second: the driving of the Fire that emerges from the bottom of Aden to people near the establishment of the Hour to the place of gathering. And two in the Hereafter: first: gathering them to the station after reviving them. And the second: turning them away from the station to Paradise or the Fire. His saying: 'called an-Nashr': that is, al-Hashr is the driving, and an-Nashr is the extraction from the graves, and this is one of two opinions, and the other is that they are the same, and that they are a name for extraction from the graves along with the driving.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the creedal obligation of believing in al-Hashr (the Gathering). It begins by defining al-Hashr as the gathering of bodies after resurrection from the graves, specifically their being driven to the station known as al-Hashr. The author then comments on the hadith about seventy thousand who enter Paradise without account, explaining that they are called 'the freed ones of ar-Rahman' and that there is a narration adding a thousand with each of them. Next, he clarifies that the first to emerge from the graves will be the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, then his two companions (Abu Bakr and 'Umar), then the people of al-Baqi', then the people of Mecca, then the people of Syria, then the rest. The passage then describes the varying ranks of people in the Gathering: some riding, some walking, some crawling on their faces, depending on their deeds. The author then enumerates the four types of al-Hashr: two in this world (the expulsion of Jews from Medina to Syria, and the driving of the Fire from Aden to the place of gathering near the Hour) and two in the Hereafter (gathering to the station after revival, and turning them from the station to Paradise or Hell). Finally, he distinguishes between al-Hashr (the driving) and an-Nashr (the extraction from graves), noting two opinions: either they are distinct or synonymous. The passage follows the order of definition, elaboration on hadith, order of emergence, states of people, types, and terminological clarification.

Types of al-Hashr and the Distinction from an-Nashr
Enumeration of four types of al-Hashr and terminological clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of al-Hashr (two in this world and two in the Hereafter) and the difference of opinion on whether al-Hashr and an-Nashr are distinct or synonymous.

Core Idea
Al-Hashr has two worldly and two otherworldly types; its relation to an-Nashr is debated.

Al-Sawi continues his exposition by enumerating the four types of al-Hashr, dividing them into those that occur in this world and those that occur in the Hereafter. He then addresses the terminological relationship between al-Hashr and an-Nashr, presenting two scholarly opinions: one distinguishes them as driving vs. extraction, while the other considers them synonymous for the entire process of resurrection and gathering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of al-Hashr
The passage states that al-Hashr has four types: two in this world (worldly) and two in the Hereafter (otherworldly). Read each type carefully.
The first worldly type is the expulsion of Jews from Medina to Syria by the Prophet ﷺ. The second is the driving of the Fire from the bottom of Aden to the gathering place near the Hour.
2
Understand the two otherworldly types
The first Hereafter type is the gathering of people to the station after they are revived. The second is the turning away of people from the station to Paradise or Hell.
These two types complete the process of judgment and final destination.
3
Learn the two opinions on al-Hashr vs. an-Nashr
The passage clarifies: one opinion says al-Hashr is the driving (sawq) and an-Nashr is the extraction (ikhraj) from graves. The other opinion says they are identical, both referring to extraction with driving.
This shows that theological terms can have nuanced definitions among scholars.
4
Reflect on the significance of these distinctions
Consider why these distinctions matter for creed. They shape how we understand the sequence of events on the Day of Judgment and the precise meanings of revealed terms.
Scholarly attention to such details reflects the depth of Islamic theology.

Al-Nashr and Al-Hashr: Definitions and Two Opinions
Definition of al-nashr and contrast with al-hashr, presenting two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between al-nashr and al-hashr according to classical Sunni theology, and be aware of the two scholarly opinions on their relationship.

Core Idea
Al-nashr is the resurrection from graves, while al-hashr is the driving to judgment — but they may be one event.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two key terms of the resurrection: al-nashr and al-hashr. While often used interchangeably, there is a nuanced difference. You'll see the two opinions held by scholars on whether they are distinct or unified.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the terms.
Pay attention to the definitions given for each term.
2
Identify the two terms and their definitions
The passage first defines al-hashr as 'driving' and al-nashr as 'bringing out from graves'. Notice how these are presented as one opinion.
The second opinion equates them.
3
Contrast the two opinions
Compare the first opinion (distinct) with the second (united). Think about how this affects theological discussions on resurrection.
This is a subtle but important point in Islamic creed.

قوله : (المسمى بالنَّشر ) : أي : فالحشر السوق، والنشر الإخراج من القبور، وهو أحد قولين والآخر أنَّهما متَحدَّان، وأنَّهما اسم للإخراج من القبور مع السُّوق .

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-nashr' in the context of resurrection. The commentator explains that al-hashr (gathering) refers to the driving (sawq) of people, while al-nashr is the bringing out (ikhraj) from the graves. He then presents two opinions: one distinguishes between the two terms, and the other considers them synonymous, treating both as referring to the bringing out from graves along with the driving.

Three Categories of People in the Resurrection
Author classifies resurrection states with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of people on the Day of Resurrection as described in the hadith and the wisdom behind this classification.

Core Idea
The resurrection reveals divine justice: people are gathered in three forms corresponding to their deeds.

The author presents a brief but profound classification of how people will be resurrected. Building on the general concept of resurrection, this passage provides specific details from the Prophet's ﷺ own words, dividing humanity into three distinct groups. This serves as a reminder of the diverse states people will experience based on their actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice the three categories mentioned.
2
Identify the three types
From the hadith, list the three types: walking, riding, on their faces. Consider what each might signify.
3
Reflect on the question and answer
Read the question asked to the Prophet and his answer. What does it teach us about Allah's power and the nature of resurrection?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومراتب الناس في الحشر متفاوتة : فمنهم الرَّاكب، ومنهم الماشي على رجليه ومنهم من يمشي على وجهه (1) أخرج الترمذي (۳۱٤٢) عن أبي هريرة الله قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يحشر الناس يوم القيامة ثلاثة أصناف: صنفاً مشاة، وصنفاً ركباناً، وصنفاً على وجوههم قيل يا رسول الله ؛ وكيف يمشون على وجوههم؟ قال : إن الذي أمشاهم على وجوههم، إنهم يتقون بوجوههم كل حدب وشوكة.
And the ranks of people in the resurrection are varying: among them are riders, among them are walkers on their feet, and among them are those who walk on their faces. (1) Al-Tirmidhi (3142) related from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah (SAW) said: 'People will be gathered on the Day of Resurrection in three types: a type walking, a type riding, and a type on their faces.' It was said: O Messenger of Allah, how will they walk on their faces? He said: 'The One who made them walk on their feet is able to make them walk on their faces, indeed they will protect themselves from every hill and thorn.'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of people's states during the resurrection (al-hashr). The author states that people have varying ranks: some ride, some walk on foot, and some crawl on their faces. He then provides a hadith from al-Tirmidhi as evidence. In the hadith, the Prophet (SAW) categorizes people into three groups: walking, riding, and on their faces. When asked how it is possible to walk on faces, the Prophet explains that the same God who made them walk on feet can make them walk on faces, and they will use their faces to shield themselves from obstacles.

Correction: The hadith shows three distinct types, indicating diversity based on deeds.

D) There is only one type.

Key Takeaway
People will be resurrected in three forms: walking, riding, or on their faces, reflecting their deeds and demonstrating Allah's absolute power.

Resurrection Forms for Adulterers, Bribe-takers, Unjust Judges, and the Self-Amazed
Enumeration of four resurrection forms corresponding to sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific forms that adulterers, consumers of bribes, unjust judges, and self-amazed individuals will take in the resurrection, and how each form corresponds to their sin.

Core Idea
Sin shapes the resurrection body; adultery, bribery, injustice, and self-conceit each have a distinct physical form.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary begins the detailed list of forms people will assume on the Day of Resurrection. It starts with four categories: those who commit adultery become monkeys, those who consume illicit earnings become pigs, the unjust judge becomes blind, and the one amazed by his own action becomes deaf and dumb. Each form is a direct consequence of the sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the structure and the terms used for each sin and form.
2
Identify each sin-form pair
List the four sins and their corresponding forms as mentioned in the passage.
3
Reflect on the correspondence
Think about why a particular form (e.g., monkey) is associated with a specific sin (adultery).
The forms are literal manifestations of the spiritual state.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test your retention.

ويكون في صُوَر مختلفة على حسب الأعمال؛ فمنهم: من هو على صورة القردة، وهم الزناة، ومنهم من هو على صورة الخنازير، آكلون السُّحت والمكس، ومنهم: الأعمى، وهو الجائر في الحكم، ومنهم : الأصمُّ ،والأبكم، وهو الذي يُعجب بفعله، ومنهم: من يمضع لسانه مُدْلَعاً على صدره يسيل القيح من فمه، وهم الوعاظ الذين تخالف أفعالهم أقوالهم، ومنهم المقطوع الأيدي والأرجُلِ، وهم الذين يؤذون الجيران، ومنهم من يصلب على جذوع من النَّار، السُّعاة بالنَّاس إلى السُّلطان، ومنهم : من هو أشدُّ نَتَناً من الجِيف، وهم الذين يُقبلون على الشهوات واللذات ويمنعون حقَّ الله من أموالهم، ومنهم من يُلبس جُبَّة سابغة من قطران لاصقة بجلده، وهم أهل الكبر والعُجب والخيلاء، كذا رأيته بخط شيخنا ناقلاً له عن الثعلبي (1) قوله :(مدلعاً) أي مدليّ. قوله : (وهم الذين يقبلون على الشهوات واللذات) : أي : المحرَّمة. (قوله بخط شيخنا المراد به العلامة العدوي نفعنا الله به . (1) الكشف والبيان (١٠ / ١١٥).
And they will be in various forms according to deeds. Among them are those in the form of monkeys, and they are the adulterers; among them are those in the form of pigs, consumers of bribes and illegal taxes; among them is the blind, and he is the unjust judge; among them is the deaf and dumb, and he is the one amazed by his own action; among them is one who chews his tongue hanging out on his chest, pus flowing from his mouth, and they are the preachers whose actions contradict their words; among them is one with severed hands and feet, and they are those who harm neighbors; among them is one crucified on trunks of fire, the slanderers who bring people to the ruler; among them is one more putrid than corpses, and they are those who turn to pleasures and lusts and withhold God's right from their wealth; among them is one who wears a long cloak of tar sticking to his skin, and they are the proud, self-conceited, and arrogant. Thus I saw it in the handwriting of our teacher, transmitted from al-Tha'labi. (1) His saying: 'hanging out' meaning protruding. His saying: 'and they are those who turn to pleasures and lusts' meaning forbidden ones. (His saying: 'in the handwriting of our teacher' meaning al-Allama al-Adawi, may God benefit us with him.) (1) al-Kashf wa al-Bayan (10/115).
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage provides an extensive list of the various physical forms that people will assume in the resurrection, each corresponding to their specific sins in worldly life. It enumerates distinct categories: adulterers become monkeys; those who consume illicit earnings (suhut and maks) become pigs; unjust judges are blind; those amazed by their own actions are deaf and dumb; hypocritical preachers have tongues hanging out with pus; those who harm neighbors have severed limbs; slanderers are crucified on fire; those who indulge in forbidden pleasures and withhold God's rights are extremely putrid; the arrogant wear a tar cloak. The commentator notes he saw this list in his teacher's handwriting, attributed to al-Tha'labi, and then provides glosses: explaining 'mudla'an' as protruding, clarifying that 'pleasures and lusts' refer to forbidden ones, and identifying 'shaykhuna' as al-Allama al-Adawi.

Correction: The passage presents them as real physical transformations that will occur on the Day of Resurrection, based on the deeds committed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You may not commit adultery or take bribes, but do you ever feel self-amazed by your own deeds? That exact attitude leads to deafness and muteness in the Hereafter. What one action you are proud of will you now re-evaluate for sincerity?
?
Have you ever passed a judgment—even in your heart—that was unfair to someone? The unjust judge becomes blind. How can you rectify that judgment today before you stand before Allah?

Resurrection Forms for Hypocritical Preachers, Harmers of Neighbors, Slanderers, the Indulgent, and the Arrogant
Enumeration of five resurrection forms corresponding to sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific forms that hypocritical preachers, those who harm neighbors, slanderers, those who indulge in forbidden pleasures while withholding God's rights, and the arrogant will take in the resurrection.

Core Idea
Outward form in the resurrection reveals inward corruption; hypocrisy, harm, slander, indulgence, and pride are visibly manifested.

This passage continues the enumeration of resurrection forms, covering five categories: preachers whose actions contradict their words have a hanging tongue with pus; those who harm neighbors have severed limbs; slanderers are crucified on fire; those who indulge in forbidden pleasures and withhold God's rights become extremely putrid; and the arrogant wear a cloak of tar. The passage also includes a gloss from al-Tha'labi.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the five categories and the descriptive details.
Pay attention to the commentator's explanatory notes at the end.
2
Match each sin to its form
Identify the specific form for each sin: hypocritical preachers, harmers of neighbors, slanderers, indulgent/withholders, arrogant.
3
Study the commentator's gloss
Read the notes: 'mudla'an' means protruding, and 'pleasures and lusts' refer to forbidden ones.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the material.

Punishment in the Grave and Beyond
Enumeration of punishment locations and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Islamic doctrine teaches that punishment for sins and disbelief occurs in multiple stages (grave, gathering, and beyond) and varies in type according to deeds.

Core Idea
Punishment is not only in Hell; it begins in the grave and at the gathering.

This passage from the text on belief in reward and punishment explains the types of punishment that await sinners. It emphasizes that punishment is not confined to Hell but occurs in the grave and on the Day of Gathering, tailored to each person's deeds. Though the passage is cut short, it offers a glimpse into the tangible forms of divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note that the author begins with the general principle before listing examples.
The passage is incomplete; the final example is cut off.
2
Identify the locations of punishment
Find the three locations where punishment occurs according to the passage: the grave, the gathering place, and after that.
This shows that punishment is not limited to Hell.
3
Examine the examples given
The passage lists three types of punishment: snakes, scorpions, and beating. Notice that the list is incomplete, indicating there are more types.
These examples are meant to be representative.
4
Reflect on the principle of justice
Punishment varies according to deeds. This reinforces the idea of individual accountability and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالثواب والعقاب (والعقاب) على الذنوب والكفر في القبر وفي المحشر وبعده بأنواع مختلفة على حسب الأعمال فمنهم: من يعاقب بالحيَّات أو بالعقارب، ومنهم من يعاقب بالضرب ومنهم: من يعاقب بغير
Belief in reward and punishment. (And punishment) for sins and disbelief is in the grave, in the gathering place, and after it, in various types according to deeds. Among them are those punished with snakes or scorpions, among them are those punished with beating, and among them are those punished with other...
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage discusses the Islamic doctrine of belief in reward and punishment (al-thawab wa al-'iqab). It asserts that punishment for sins and disbelief occurs in multiple stages: in the grave (al-qabr), in the gathering place (al-mahshar), and after that. The punishment varies in type according to the individual's deeds. The passage then begins to enumerate examples: some are punished with snakes (hayyat) or scorpions ('aqarib), some with beating (darb), and some with other unspecified forms. The text is cut off at the end of the page, leaving the list incomplete.

Specific Punishments for Sin in the Grave and Afterlife
Al-Sawi enumerates sinners and their specific punishments in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic tradition (as transmitted by al-Tha'labi) describes specific punishments tailored to particular sins in the grave and afterlife.

Core Idea
Each sin receives a fitting punishment.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary presents a vivid description of punishments in the grave and the hereafter, based on a tradition from al-Tha'labi. The punishments are not arbitrary but correspond to the nature of the sins committed. As you read, notice how each affliction reflects the spiritual corruption of the sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own enumeration come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the repetition of 'وهم' (and they are) which introduces each category.
2
Identify the two categories of punishment
The passage contains two lists: the first describes punishments by transformation (e.g., becoming monkeys, pigs), and the second describes punishments by torment (e.g., snakes, scorpions, beating). Notice how each list begins with a heading.
The first list starts with 'الإيمان بالثواب والعقاب' and the second list repeats the same phrase.
3
Match each sin with its punishment
Go through the first list and for each sin mentioned (adultery, illicit gain, unjust judgment, etc.), identify the corresponding punishment form. Then move to the second list and note the general types of torment.
Some punishments are vivid metaphors, but the text treats them as literal.
4
Reflect on the principle of divine justice
Consider why each punishment fits the sin. For example, adulterers become monkeys – why? Reflect on how the punishment reflects the spiritual condition. This is a key theme in Islamic theology: reward and punishment are tailor-made.
This lesson is not about debating literalness but understanding the tradition's internal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالثواب والعقاب
وهم
على صورة القردة، وهم الزناة، ومنهم من هو على صورة الخنازير، آكلون السُّحت والمكس، ومنهم: الأعمى، وهو الجائر في الحكم، ومنهم : الأصمُّ ،والأبكم، وهو الذي يُعجب بفعله، ومنهم: من يمضع لسانه مُدْلَعاً على صدره يسيل القيح من فمه، وهم الوعاظ الذين تخالف أفعالهم أقوالهم، ومنهم المقطوع الأيدي والأرجُلِ، وهم الذين يؤذون الجيران، ومنهم من يصلب على جذوع من النَّار، السُّعاة بالنَّاس إلى السُّلطان، ومنهم : من هو أشدُّ نَتَناً من الجِيف، وهم الذين يُقبلون على الشهوات واللذات ويمنعون حقَّ الله من أموالهم، ومنهم من يُلبس جُبَّة سابغة من قطران لاصقة بجلده، وهم أهل الكبر والعُجب والخيلاء، كذا رأيته بخط شيخنا ناقلاً له عن
وهم
الثعلبي
الإيمان بالثواب والعقاب
(والعقاب) على الذنوب والكفر في القبر وفي المحشر وبعده بأنواع مختلفة على حسب الأعمال فمنهم: من يعاقب بالحيَّات أو
بالعقارب، ومنهم من يعاقب بالضرب ومنهم: من يعاقب بغير
قوله :(مدلعاً) أي مدليّ.
قوله : (وهم الذين يقبلون على الشهوات واللذات) : أي : المحرَّمة.
(قوله بخط شيخنا المراد به العلامة العدوي نفعنا الله به .
Belief in reward and punishment — and they are in the form of monkeys, and they are the adulterers; and among them are those in the form of pigs, eaters of illicit gain and bribes; and among them: the blind, and he is the unjust judge; and among them: the deaf and dumb, and he is the one who is amazed by his own action; and among them: one who chews his tongue protruding on his chest, pus flowing from his mouth, and they are the preachers whose actions contradict their words; and among them: those with amputated hands and feet, and they are those who harm neighbors; and among them: those crucified on trunks of fire, the slanderers who carry tales to the ruler; and among them: one more foul-smelling than corpses, and they are those who indulge in desires and pleasures and withhold God's right from their wealth; and among them: one who wears a long cloak of tar sticking to his skin, and they are the people of pride, arrogance, and haughtiness. Thus I saw it in the handwriting of our teacher, quoting it from al-Tha'labi. Belief in reward and punishment — and punishment for sins and disbelief in the grave, in the gathering, and after it in various kinds according to deeds: among them are those punished by snakes or scorpions, among them those punished by beating, and among them those punished by other than that. His saying: (protruding) i.e., hanging down. His saying: (and they are those who indulge in desires and pleasures) i.e., the forbidden. (His saying: 'in the handwriting of our teacher' meaning the scholar al-'Adawi, may Allah benefit us with him).
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration from al-Tha'labi, transmitted by al-Sawi, listing specific punishments for various sins in the grave and the afterlife. It begins with the heading 'Belief in reward and punishment' and then presents two lists. The first list associates each sin with a particular form or affliction: adulterers become monkeys, consumers of illicit gain become pigs, unjust judges are blind, hypocritical preachers chew their tongues with pus, those who harm neighbors have amputated limbs, slanderers are crucified on fire, those who indulge in forbidden pleasures become foul-smelling, and the proud wear a tar cloak. The second list mentions punishments by snakes, scorpions, beating, and others, varying according to deeds. The commentary then defines key words: 'mudla'an' (protruding) and clarifies that the desires and pleasures refer to forbidden ones. Finally, it identifies 'our teacher' as al-'Adawi.

Misconception: These physical descriptions are merely metaphorical or symbolic.
Correction: The text presents them as literal punishments that will occur in the grave and afterlife, as transmitted from al-Tha'labi. While some interpretations may allow metaphor, the passage at face value describes actual states.

?
