The Reality and Number of the Prophet's Pool
Describes the controversy over the number and location of the Prophet's pool, and its description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about the number of pools (one or two), the location of the Prophet's pool relative to the Sirat, and the relationship between the Hawd and the river Kawthar.

Core Idea
The Prophet's pool is a single body before the Sirat; Kawthar is a river that feeds it.

This lesson addresses a key eschatological belief: the Hawd (Pool) of the Prophet Muhammad. Scholars debate whether there is one pool or two, and whether one is after the Sirat. The correct view is that the pool is before the Sirat, and the after-Sirat body is actually the river Kawthar. Let's explore the details from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the pool's reality
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question about one or two pools.
The passage immediately presents a scholarly dispute.
2
Understand the debate and resolution
Identify the two opinions and which one is preferred. Note that the second pool after the Sirat is identified as al-Kawthar, a river.
3
Note the description and promise
Observe how the pool is described: a specific body fed by two spouts from Kawthar. Understand the promise that whoever drinks will never thirst again.
This promise highlights the pool's unique blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل هو حوض واحد أو حوضان، والثاني بعد الصراط؟ قولان، وقيل : الذي بعد الصِّراط هو الكوثر ، وهو نهر في الجَنَّة لا حوض، وإنَّما الحوض قبل الصراط، وهو جسم مخصوص يصب فيه ميزابان ماء الكوثر، تَرِدُه أمَّتُه عليه الصَّلاة والسّلام، من شرب منه شربة لا يظمأ بعدها أبداً . ويكون الشّرب في الجنَّة، إنَّما هو على سبيل التَّللُّذ لا العطش، ويُطرد عنه من بدَّل وغَيَّر، إمّا بالارتداد وإما أن يُحدِث في الدِّين ليس منه؛ كأهل البدع على اختلاف أنواعهم، وكأهل الكبائر المعلنين بها، وكالظلمة الجائرين في أحكامهم؛ لأنَّ المرتد مخلد في النار، وخالف المعتزلة في ذلك، وهم أحقُّ للطرد عنه من غيرهم. قوله : (ترده أمَّته أي والأمين عليه علي بن أبي طالب كما ورد. قوله : (لا يظمأ بعدها أبداً أي ولو دخل النَّار؛ فلا يعذَّب فيها بالعطش. قوله : ( ويطرد عنه من بدل وغير أي فالكافر لا يشرب منه، والمبتدع يشرب منه بعد الرَّد.
And is it one pool or two pools, the second after the Sirat? Two statements, and it is said: that which is after the Sirat is al-Kawthar, and it is a river in Paradise, not a pool. The pool is only before the Sirat, and it is a specific body into which two spouts pour water from al-Kawthar. His nation (upon him prayer and peace) will come to it; whoever drinks from it a drink will never thirst after it ever. And drinking in Paradise is only for pleasure, not due to thirst. And he is repelled from it who changed and altered, either by apostasy or by introducing into the religion what is not from it; like the people of innovation in all their types, and like the people of major sins who are open about them, and like the oppressive tyrants in their judgments. Because the apostate is eternal in the Fire. And the Mu'tazila disagreed in that, and they are more deserving of being repelled from it than others. His statement: (His nation will come to it) meaning, and the trustee over it is Ali ibn Abi Talib as has been reported. His statement: (he will never thirst after it ever) meaning, even if he enters the Fire; he will not be punished therein with thirst. His statement: (and he is repelled from it who changed and altered) meaning, the disbeliever does not drink from it, and the innovator drinks from it after the rejection.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses the Islamic eschatological belief in the Hawd (Pool) of the Prophet Muhammad. It begins by noting a scholarly debate: is there one pool or two? Some say the second after the Sirat is actually al-Kawthar, which is a river in Paradise, not a pool. The actual pool is before the Sirat. The pool is described as a specific physical body into which two spouts pour water from al-Kawthar. The Prophet's community will come to this pool. Whoever drinks from it once will never feel thirst again. Drinking in Paradise is for pleasure, not from need. Then the passage explains who is repelled from the pool: those who change or alter the religion, whether by apostasy or by introducing innovations. This includes all types of innovators, open sinners, and unjust rulers. Apostates are condemned to eternal fire. The Mu'tazila are singled out as disagreeing on this point, and the author asserts that they are more deserving of being repelled than others. The commentary then clarifies specific phrases from the main text: 'his nation will come to it' indicates that the custodian of the pool is Ali ibn Abi Talib; 'he will never thirst after it' means even if a person enters Hell, they will not suffer thirst; and 'who changed and altered' means the disbeliever is completely prevented, while the innovator may drink after being repulsed (possibly meaning after repentance).

Key Terms
حوض
كوثر
الصراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Kawthar is the Pool (Hawd) itself.
Correction: Al-Kawthar is actually a river in Paradise that supplies the pool; the pool is a separate body before the Sirat.

Checkpoint
Where is the Prophet's pool located according to the passage?
A) After the Sirat
B) Before the Sirat
C) In Paradise after the bridge
D) On the Day of Judgment

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's pool (Hawd) is a single, specific body of water before the Sirat, fed by the Kawthar river, from which believers drink and never thirst again.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to drink from the Prophet's pool? What worldly thirsts occupy your heart that distract you from preparing for that eternal drink? Commit today to one action that draws you closer to that hope.
?
The water of the Hawd quenches all thirst forever. What insatiable desire in your life are you trying to satisfy apart from Allah? How can you redirect that longing toward the promise of this pool?
?
If you knew you would stand before the Prophet's pool tomorrow, what would you change today? Examine your deeds and repentance: are you sincerely striving to be among those who drink? Ask Allah to grant you that blessed drink.

Who is Repelled from the Prophet's Pool?

Belief in the Createdness of Paradise and Hell
Defines belief in the createdness of Paradise and Hell and explains the term 'al-niran'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Paradise and Hell are already created entities, and the technical meaning of 'al-niran' as a subtle burning body associated with the Sirat and the Balance.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell are real, created now, and not merely future.

This opening establishes a foundational eschatological belief: the ultimate abodes of reward and punishment are not awaiting creation but exist at this very moment. The author then clarifies the term 'al-niran' (the fires), defining it as a subtle burning body that rises upward, connected to the Sirat and the Balance. Importantly, a delay in grasping this connection does not harm one's faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic sentence that begins with 'ما الإيمان بالجنّة والنار...'. Notice it is phrased as a question or definition of belief.
2
Understand the createdness
The text explicitly states that Paradise and Fire are created now (مخلوقتان الآن). Ponder why this is an article of faith.
This refutes those who say they will be created later.
3
Define 'al-niran'
The term is defined: it is a subtle burning body that tends upwards. Link it to the Sirat and the Balance.
The exact relationship may be left open, as delay does not harm belief.
4
Reflect on the leniency
Note the phrase 'or its delay does not harm belief', showing that not all details need to be understood for sound faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما الإيمان بالجنّة ،والنّار، وأنّهما مخلوقتان الآن (والنيران) بكسر النّون جمع نار وهي جسم لطيف محرق يميل إلى جهة العُلوّ، والمراد بها الصراط والميزان أو تأخره لا يضرُّ في الاعتقاد. الإيمان بالجنة والنار دار العقاب الذي أشدُّه النار بجميع طبقاتها السبع، أعلاها جهنَّم وهي لعصاة المؤمنين، ثمّ تخرب بعد خروجهم منها، فلظَى، فالحطمة، فالسَّعير، فَسَقَر فالجَحِيم، فالهاوية، وباب كل من داخل الأخرى على الاستواء. وحرُّها هواء مُحرق، لا جمر لها سوى بني آدم والجنّ والأحجار المتخذَة آلهةً من دون الله ، نعوذ بالله منها . قوله : (دار العقاب) ورد في وصفها (١) : أنَّ أرضها من رصاص. وسقفها من نحاس، حيطانها من كبريت وقودها النَّاس والحجارة (٢) .
What is belief in Paradise and the Fire, and that they are created now. (And the fires) with a kasra on the noon, plural of fire, and it is a subtle burning body that tends upwards, and what is meant by it are the Sirat and the Balance, or its delay does not harm belief. Belief in Paradise and the Fire: the abode of punishment the most intense of which is the Fire with all its seven layers, the highest of which is Jahannam, and it is for the sinful believers, then it becomes ruined after their exit from it. Then Lazza, then al-Hutama, then al-Sa'ir, then Saqar, then al-Jahim, then al-Hawiya. And the door of each is inside the other on a level. And its heat is burning air, it has no embers except the sons of Adam and the jinn and the stones taken as gods besides Allah; we seek refuge in Allah from it. His statement: (the abode of punishment) has come in its description (1): that its earth is of lead, its ceiling of copper, its walls of sulfur, its fuel people and stones (2).
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers the Islamic belief in Paradise and Hell, emphasizing that both are created entities existing now. It defines 'al-niran' (fires) as a plural of 'nar', which is a subtle burning body that rises upward. The reference to 'al-Sirat' and 'al-Mizan' suggests that these fires are associated with the bridge and the scales, though delay in understanding does not harm belief. The core of the passage describes the seven layers of Hell (the abode of punishment). The highest layer is Jahannam, which houses sinful believers; it will be destroyed after they leave it. The subsequent layers are: Lazza (for Jews), al-Hutama (for Christians), al-Sa'ir (for the Sabians), Saqar (for Magi), al-Jahim (for idol worshippers), and al-Hawiya (for hypocrites and extreme disbelievers like Pharaoh). Each layer's door is contained within the next, uniformly. Then it describes Hell's heat as burning air, with no fuel except humans, jinn, and idols of stone. Finally, a commentary on the phrase 'abode of punishment' quotes a description: its earth is lead, its ceiling copper, its walls sulfur, and its fuel people and stones.

Key Terms
النيران
الصراط
الميزان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Paradise and Hell are only created after the Day of Judgment.
Correction: The text clearly states they are created now (مخلوقتان الآن).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-niran' mean?
A) A subtle burning body that tends upwards
B) The fuel of Hell
C) The angels of punishment
D) The gates of Heaven

Key Takeaway
Paradise and Hell are real, created entities that exist now, and the term 'al-niran' refers to a subtle burning body associated with the Sirat and the Balance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Seven Levels of Hell and Their Inhabitants
Enumerates the seven layers of Hell, their arrangement, and describes its heat and fuel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the names of the seven layers of Hell in order, which layer is for sinful believers, and the description of Hell's heat and fuel.

Core Idea
Hell has seven layers, each inside the other, with Jahannam as the highest for sinful Muslims.

After establishing the createdness of Hell, the author provides a detailed enumeration of its seven layers. The greatest punishment is the Fire itself, which comprises these layers. The highest layer, Jahannam, is designated for sinful believers from the Muslim community, and it will be destroyed after they leave. The subsequent layers house other disbelievers, though their specific inhabitants are not named here. The passage also describes Hell's unique heat and fuel.

Guided Path
1
List the seven layers
Read the names in order: Jahannam, Lazza, Hutama, Sa'ir, Saqar, Jahim, Hawiya. Memorize the sequence.
2
Understand Jahannam's inhabitants
The text says Jahannam is for the sinful believers (عصاة المؤمنين) and that it will be ruined after they exit.
This implies their punishment is temporary.
3
Note the spatial arrangement
The door of each layer is inside the other on a level, indicating they are nested.
4
Reflect on Hell's nature
Its heat is burning air, and its only embers are humans, jinn, and stone idols. This shows the physical reality of the punishment.
5
Read the extra description
The final part quotes a description of the materials: earth of lead, ceiling of copper, walls of sulfur, fuel people and stones.
This adds to the vivid picture.

(And the fires) with a kasra on the noon, plural of fire, and it is a subtle burning body that tends upwards, and what is meant by it are the Sirat and the Balance, or its delay does not harm belief. Belief in Paradise and the Fire: the abode of punishment the most intense of which is the Fire with all its seven layers, the highest of which is Jahannam, and it is for the sinful believers, then it becomes ruined after their exit from it. Then Lazza, then al-Hutama, then al-Sa'ir, then Saqar, then al-Jahim, then al-Hawiya. And the door of each is inside the other on a level. And its heat is burning air, it has no embers except the sons of Adam and the jinn and the stones taken as gods besides Allah; we seek refuge in Allah from it. His statement: (the abode of punishment) has come in its description (1): that its earth is of lead, its ceiling of copper, its walls of sulfur, its fuel people and stones (2).
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage covers the Islamic belief in Paradise and Hell, emphasizing that both are created entities existing now. It defines 'al-niran' (fires) as a plural of 'nar', which is a subtle burning body that rises upward. The reference to 'al-Sirat' and 'al-Mizan' suggests that these fires are associated with the bridge and the scales, though delay in understanding does not harm belief. The core of the passage describes the seven layers of Hell (the abode of punishment). The highest layer is Jahannam, which houses sinful believers; it will be destroyed after they leave it. The subsequent layers are: Lazza (for Jews), al-Hutama (for Christians), al-Sa'ir (for the Sabians), Saqar (for Magi), al-Jahim (for idol worshippers), and al-Hawiya (for hypocrites and extreme disbelievers like Pharaoh). Each layer's door is contained within the next, uniformly. Then it describes Hell's heat as burning air, with no fuel except humans, jinn, and idols of stone. Finally, a commentary on the phrase 'abode of punishment' quotes a description: its earth is lead, its ceiling copper, its walls sulfur, and its fuel people and stones.

Key Terms

حطمة
سعير
سقر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All levels of Hell are eternal for all inhabitants.
Correction: Jahannam is for sinful believers and will be ruined after they leave, so it is not eternal for them.

Checkpoint
Which level of Hell is designated for the sinful believers (عصاة المؤمنين)?
A) جهنّم (Jahannam)
B) لظى (Lazza)
C) سقر (Saqar)
D) الهاوية (al-Hawiya)

Key Takeaway
Hell consists of seven nested layers, with Jahannam as the first (highest) for sinful believers, and its description includes burning air with human and idol fuel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The stones taken as gods become fuel for the Fire. What 'gods' have you set up in your life—money, status, relationships—that might become the very fuel that torments you? How can you begin to tear them down today?
?
The layers of Hell are nested, each door inside the other. Are you aware of how your small sins drag you deeper into the levels of punishment? Where are you right now in this descent, and what step will you take to ascend back to safety?

Definition of Jannah: Linguistic Meaning and Textual Caution
Gives linguistic definition then mistakenly inserts Hell description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and terminological definition of Jannah (Paradise) in Islamic theology, and recognize a common textual confusion with descriptions of Hell.

Core Idea
Jannah means garden; its true description is not lead and sulfur.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of Jannah (Paradise) as understood in the Kharidah tradition. The passage begins correctly with the linguistic meaning, but then includes a description that scholars recognize as belonging to Hell, not Paradise. We will clarify the proper definition and note the textual issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the abrupt shift from the linguistic definition to a description of punishment.
The passage seems to mix two different topics.
2
Identify the linguistic definition
The first part clearly defines Jannah linguistically as 'al-bustan' (the garden). This is the core meaning.
3
Recognize the textual error
The description of lead, copper, sulfur, and fuel of people and stones is actually for Hell (Jahannam), not Paradise. The phrase 'Dar al-Aqab' (abode of punishment) is a title of Hell.
This is a known corruption in some manuscripts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لغة : البستان، والمراد منها (والجنان) جمع جنَّة، وهي قوله : (دار العقاب) ورد في وصفها (١) : أنَّ أرضها من رصاص. وسقفها من نحاس، حيطانها من كبريت وقودها النَّاس والحجارة (٢) .
Linguistically: the garden. And what is meant by it (and the gardens) plural of garden, and it is His statement: (the abode of punishment) has come in its description (1): that its earth is of lead, its ceiling of copper, its walls of sulfur, its fuel people and stones (2).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage begins by defining 'Jannah' linguistically as 'al-bustan' (the garden). The term 'al-Jinan' (the gardens) is the plural of Jannah. However, the text then immediately says 'and it is His statement: (the abode of punishment)' which appears to be a misplacement, as the 'abode of punishment' is Hell, not Paradise. The description that follows (earth of lead, ceiling of copper, walls of sulfur, fuel of people and stones) matches the earlier description of Hell, not Paradise. Therefore, this passage likely contains a textual corruption or confusion. The intended definition of Jannah may have been interrupted by a commentary on the phrase 'Dar al-Aqab' from the previous lesson. As it stands, the passage is problematic and requires scholarly clarification.

Key Terms
جنّة
جنان
دار العقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The description of Paradise includes lead, copper, sulfur, and fuel of people and stones.
Correction: That description actually belongs to Hell (Jahannam), not Paradise (Jannah). The passage has an interpolation.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic meaning of 'Jannah'?
A) Garden
B) Fire
C) Abode
D) Pool

Key Takeaway
Jannah linguistically means garden, and its true nature is bliss, not punishment; the description of lead and sulfur refers to Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single scribal error can confuse the description of Paradise and Hell, how careful are you in ensuring that the source of your knowledge about the Hereafter is sound? Will you commit to seeking knowledge from reliable texts and teachers?
?
When you read the description of Hell (lead, copper, sulfur, people and stones as fuel), did your heart tremble? Have you truly internalized the reality of Hellfire, or do you let these descriptions become mere words that pass over your ears without affecting your actions?
?
The lesson shows how a textual mistake can lead to a false understanding of Jannah. In your own life, what 'mistaken insertions' have you allowed to distort your understanding of faith? How will you purify your beliefs by returning to the original sources?

The Seven Layers of Hell and Their Inhabitants
Enumerates the seven layers of Hell, their descriptions, and the groups assigned to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the names, order, and assigned groups of the seven layers of Hell, as well as the special status of Jahannam for sinful Muslims.

Core Idea
Hell has seven layers, each for a specific type of sinner, but Jahannam is unique for sinful Muslims and will be destroyed.

In Islamic eschatology, Hell (Jahannam) is described as having multiple layers of punishment. This passage from al-Kharida enumerates the seven layers by name, notes their physical features, and assigns each to a category of disbelievers or sinners. Understanding this classification helps clarify the varying degrees of punishment and the ultimate fate of sinful Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a general statement about the abode of punishment.
2
Identify the seven layers in order
List the seven layer names as they appear: Jahannam, Laza, al-Hutama, al-Sa‘ir, Saqar, al-Jahim, al-Hawiya. Notice that Jahannam is the highest and has a unique feature: it will be destroyed after sinful Muslims leave.
3
Understand the assignment of groups
Each of the six lower layers is assigned to a specific group: Laza for Jews, al-Hutama for Christians, al-Sa‘ir for Sabians, Saqar for Magi, al-Jahim for idol worshippers, al-Hawiya for hypocrites and extreme disbelievers.
The assignments reflect categories of disbelief.
4
Reflect on Jahannam's uniqueness
Jahannam is for sinful Muslims (those who committed major sins but had faith) and will be destroyed after they are purified. This contrasts with the other layers, which are eternal for their inhabitants.
This corrects the misconception that all layers are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دار العقاب الذي أشدُّه النار بجميع طبقاتها السبع، أعلاها جهنَّم وهي لعصاة المؤمنين، ثمّ تخرب بعد خروجهم منها، فلظَى، فالحطمة، فالسَّعير، فَسَقَر فالجَحِيم، فالهاوية، وباب كل من داخل الأخرى على الاستواء. ( وحرُّها هواء مُحرق، لا جمر لها سوى بني آدم والجنّ والأحجار المتخذَة آلهةً من دون الله ، نعوذ بالله منها . قوله : (دار العقاب) ورد في وصفها (١) : أنَّ أرضها من رصاص. وسقفها من نحاس، حيطانها من كبريت وقودها النَّاس والحجارة (٢) . قوله : (فلظى) : أي : وهي لليهود. قوله : (فالحطمة): وهي للنصارى. قوله: (فالسعير) وهي للصَّائبين؛ فرقة من اليهود ازدادوا ضلالاً بعبادتهم العجل . قوله : (فسقر): وهي للمجوس عبَّاد النَّار . قوله : (فالجحيم): وهي لعبدة الأصنام. قوله : (فالهاوية) وهي للمنافقين وكلُّ من اشتدَّ كفره؛ كفرعون، وهامان، وقارون. (۱) في المطبوع: (صفتها). (۲) ينظر الزهر «الفائح لابن الجزري (ص۲۲).
The abode of punishment, the most severe of which is the Fire, in all its seven layers. Its highest is Jahannam, and it is for sinful believers; then it will be destroyed after they come out of it. Then Laza, then al-Hutama, then al-Sa‘ir, then Saqar, then al-Jahim, then al-Hawiya. The door of each one enters into the other equally. And its heat is burning air; it has no embers except for the sons of Adam, jinn, and the stones taken as gods besides Allah – we seek refuge with Allah from it. His saying: (Dar al-‘Iqab) – it is mentioned in its description (1): that its ground is of lead, its ceiling of copper, its walls of sulfur, and its fuel is people and stones (2). His saying: (Laza) – that is, it is for the Jews. His saying: (al-Hutama) – it is for the Christians. His saying: (al-Sa‘ir) – it is for the Sabians, a sect of the Jews who increased in deviation by worshipping the calf. His saying: (Saqar) – it is for the Magi, worshippers of fire. His saying: (al-Jahim) – it is for idol worshippers. His saying: (al-Hawiya) – it is for the hypocrites and everyone whose disbelief is intense, like Pharaoh, Haman, and Qarun. (1) In the printed edition: (its description). (2) See al-Zahr al-Fa’ih by Ibn al-Jazari (p. 22).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage describes the seven layers of Hell (Jahannam) and assigns each layer to a particular group of sinners. It begins by stating that the abode of punishment (Dar al-‘Iqab) is the Fire in all its seven layers. The highest layer is Jahannam, which is specifically for sinful believers (those Muslims who committed major sins but died with faith). After these believers are purified and taken out, Jahannam will be destroyed. Then the passage lists the remaining six names in order: Laza, al-Hutama, al-Sa‘ir, Saqar, al-Jahim, and al-Hawiya. It notes that the door of each layer enters into the next equally, indicating a physical connection. Then a parenthetical note describes the extreme heat: it is burning air, with no fuel except humans, jinn, and the stones that were worshipped as idols. The passage then provides a separate commentary on the phrase 'Dar al-‘Iqab' from the original text, quoting a description that the ground is lead, the ceiling copper, the walls sulfur, and its fuel people and stones. Finally, each of the six lower layers is assigned to a specific group: Laza for Jews, al-Hutama for Christians, al-Sa‘ir for Sabians (a Jewish sect), Saqar for Magi (fire worshippers), al-Jahim for idol worshippers, and al-Hawiya for hypocrites and extreme disbelievers like Pharaoh, Haman, and Qarun. The passage thus provides both a physical and a punitive classification of Hell.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All layers of Hell are eternal for all inhabitants.
Correction: The passage specifies that Jahannam is for sinful Muslims who will eventually exit and then be destroyed, so it is not eternal for them and the layer itself will be destroyed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which layer of Hell is specifically for sinful Muslims and will be destroyed after they leave?
A) Laza
B) Jahannam
C) Saqar
D) al-Hawiya

Key Takeaway
Hell consists of seven layers each designated for specific categories of sinners, with Jahannam being the highest and the only layer that will be destroyed after the sinful Muslims it contains are purified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Jahannam is for sinful Muslims, does your conscience accuse you of sins you have not repented from? What are you relying on to save you—mere belief or actual obedience? Write down one sin you will sincerely turn from today.
?
The lowest layer, al-Hawiya, is for hypocrites. When you pray, fast, or give charity, do you ever seek praise from people? How can you purify your intention right now, before you face that outcome?
?
Hell's fuel includes stones worshipped as gods. What 'stones' have you set up in your heart—wealth, status, a relationship, or your own desires—that compete with Allah? Will you topple them today by an act of sacrifice?

The Seven Gardens of Paradise and a Scholarly Disagreement
Enumeration of Paradise's gardens and scholarly disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional enumeration of Paradise's gardens according to Ibn Abbas and the scholarly disagreement regarding their number.

Core Idea
The names of Paradise are either distinct gardens or different names for one essence.

This passage from al-Kharida discusses the abode of reward and lists seven gardens. It presents the opinion of Ibn Abbas and a group, then notes that the majority hold there are four, and some say it is one. As we read, we will learn both the names and the spectrum of scholarly views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the full English translation. Notice the names of the gardens and the different opinions.
Pay attention to the footnote marker in the Arabic; it refers to a verse in Surah al-Rahman.
2
Identify the seven gardens enumerated by Ibn Abbas
The passage lists seven gardens in descending order: al-Firdaws, Jannat al-Ma’wa, Jannat al-Khuld, Jannat al-Na‘im, Jannat ‘Adn, Dar al-Salam, and Dar al-Jalal. Note their names and sequence.
The highest is al-Firdaws, above which is the Throne of the Most Merciful.
3
Understand the scholarly disagreement
The passage reports three opinions: the seven-garden view of Ibn Abbas, the majority view of four gardens, and a view that Paradise is one entity with multiple names.
The majority opinion is based on a verse in Surah al-Rahman.
4
Reflect on the significance of these differences
Consider why scholars might differ on this matter. What does it tell us about the nature of theological knowledge and interpretation?
This disagreement does not affect the core belief in Paradise, but shows the richness of scholarly discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دار الثَّواب وهي سبع أعلاها وأفضلها الفردوس، وفوقها عرسُ الرَّحمن، ومنها تنفجر أنهار الجنَّة، فجنَّةُ المأوى، فجنَّةُ الخُلد، فجنَّةُ النَّعيم، فجنَّةُ عَدْن، فدارُ السَّلام، فدار الجلال، هذا ما ذهب إليه ابن عباس وجماعة. وذهب الجمهور إلى أنَّها أربع بدليل ما في سورة (الرحمن)(۱)، وقيل: الجنة واحدة، وما تقدَّم أسماء لمسمّى واحد، إذ كل اسم صالح لها .
The abode of reward – it is seven. Its highest and most excellent is al-Firdaws, and above it is the Throne of the Most Merciful, and from it burst forth the rivers of Paradise. Then Jannat al-Ma’wa, then Jannat al-Khuld, then Jannat al-Na‘im, then Jannat ‘Adn, then Dar al-Salam, then Dar al-Jalal. This is the opinion of Ibn ‘Abbas and a group. And the majority held that they are four, based on what is in Surah al-Rahman (1), and it was said: Paradise is one, and what preceded are names for one named, since each name is suitable for it.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage describes the gardens of Paradise (Dar al-Thawab). It states that there are seven gardens, with al-Firdaws being the highest and most excellent. Above al-Firdaws is the Throne of the Most Merciful. From al-Firdaws burst forth the rivers of Paradise. The remaining six gardens are named in order: Jannat al-Ma’wa (Garden of Refuge), Jannat al-Khuld (Garden of Eternity), Jannat al-Na‘im (Garden of Bliss), Jannat ‘Adn (Garden of Eden), Dar al-Salam (Abode of Peace), and Dar al-Jalal (Abode of Majesty). This enumeration is attributed to Ibn ‘Abbas and a group of scholars. However, the passage then notes a disagreement: the majority of scholars hold that there are only four gardens, based on the verse in Surah al-Rahman which mentions two gardens for those who fear their Lord (and the context implies more). Another opinion is that Paradise is actually one entity, and the various names are just different designations for the same reality, each name appropriate for describing it. The passage thus presents multiple views without adjudicating between them.

Key Terms

الفردوس
جنة المأوى
جنة الخلد
جنة النعيم
جنة عدن
دار السلام
دار الجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the names of Paradise refer to distinct, separate places without considering the possibility that they might be different names for the same Paradise.
Correction: The passage reports that some scholars hold that Paradise is one entity with multiple names, each name being appropriate for describing it.

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion mentioned in the passage, how many gardens of Paradise are there?
A) Seven
B) Four
C) One
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The number of gardens in Paradise is a matter of scholarly interpretation, with three main opinions: seven, four, or one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the highest garden is al-Firdaws, and above it is the Throne of Allah. Are you genuinely striving for the highest station, or are you satisfied with a lower aim? What specific change in your worship or character could bring you closer to reaching al-Firdaws?
?
The scholars humbly presented multiple opinions about the unseen without arrogance. Is there an area of your faith where you are certain but should be more humble? What would it cost you to admit 'Allah knows best' more often?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked, 'What did you do to seek My Paradise?' would your answer be filled with actions or excuses? What one deed can you commit to today that will tip the scale toward your entry into any of His gardens?

Hell's Names: Specific or General?
Poem on Hell's layers and critique of specific assignments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while some traditions assign specific names of Hell to specific groups, the Quran uses these names interchangeably for all disbelievers.

Core Idea
Hell's names apply to all disbelievers, not just specific groups.

This lesson presents a poem by Shaykh al-Amir that assigns each layer of Hell to a specific group of disbelievers. However, the author then critiques this view by pointing out that the Quran applies all these names to disbelievers in general. Understanding this nuance helps avoid oversimplification of Islamic eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the seven names of Hell and the groups they are assigned to.
The poem follows the tawil meter.
2
Identify each name and its assigned group
Go through the poem line by line: Jahannam for the disobedient, Laza for Jews, Hatama for Christians, Sa‘ir for Sabians, Saqar for Magi, Jahim for idol worshippers, Hawiya for hypocrites.
3
Read the author's critique
What does the author say about this assignment? He notes that the commentary follows some hadiths, but the Quran testifies that each name applies to all disbelievers.
The Quranic verses describe disbelievers' attributes with any of these names.
4
Reflect on the evidence
Ask yourself: Why would the author say this? How does the Quran use these names? Reflect on the tension between specific assignments and general application.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نظم ذلك شيخنا الأمير بقوله : من الطويل] جهنَّم للعاصي لظى ليهودها وحطمة دار للنَّصارى أولى الصَّمم سعير عذاب الصَّابئين ودارهم مجوس لها سقر جحيم لذي صنم وهاوية دار النفاق وقيتها وأسأل ربَّ العرش أمناً من النّقم وما ذكره الشَّرح تبع فيه بعض الأحاديث، ولكنَّ آيات القرآن شاهدة بأنَّ كلَّ اسم من تلك الأسماء يطلق على ما يعم الجميع؛ لأنه يذكر صفات الكفَّار بأي وجه، ويعبر عن وعيدهم بأي اسم من هذه الأسماء، فتدبَّر .
And our master al-Amir versified that, saying (from the tawil meter): 'Jahannam for the disobedient, Laza for its Jews, and Hatama a house for the Christians, the deaf; Sa‘ir – the punishment of the Sabians, and their abode; the Magi have Saqar; Jahim for the one with an idol; and Hawiya is the house of hypocrisy, and its guardian – and I ask the Lord of the Throne for security from punishments.' And what the commentary mentioned follows some hadiths therein, but the verses of the Quran testify that every one of those names is applied to what encompasses all: because it mentions the attributes of the disbelievers in any respect, and expresses their threat with any of these names, so reflect.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents a poem by Shaykh al-Amir that summarizes the assignment of Hell layers to specific groups: Jahannam for the disobedient (the sinful believers), Laza for the Jews, Hatama (al-Hutama) for the Christians, Sa‘ir for the Sabians, Saqar for the Magi, Jahim for idol worshippers, and Hawiya for the hypocrites. The poet concludes with a supplication for security. The passage then critiques this specific assignment: it acknowledges that the commentary (the sharh) follows some hadiths, but the Quranic verses testify that each of these names actually applies to all disbelievers generally. The reason given is that the Quran describes the attributes of disbelievers in various ways and threatens them with any of these names, not with a specific name for a specific group. Therefore, the assignment in the poem is not necessarily accurate. The reader is urged to ponder this.

Key Terms
جهنَّم
لظى
حطمة
سعير
سقر
جحيم
هاوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe that Hell's layers are exclusively for specific groups as in the poem.
Correction: The author warns that the Quran generalizes the names to all disbelievers; therefore, the specific assignments are not definitive.

Checkpoint
According to the author's critique, what do the verses of the Quran indicate about the names of Hell?
A) They are specific to each group of disbelievers.
B) Each name applies to all disbelievers.
C) The names are only for Jews and Christians.
D) Only Jahannam is used for sinful Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Quran uses the names of Hell interchangeably for all disbelievers, so we should not restrict them to specific groups based on certain hadiths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that a particular sin or group of people deserves Hell more than others? How does this lesson's insistence that all names of Hell apply to all disbelievers expose that hidden arrogance in your heart? What will you do today to root it out?
?
The poem ends with a supplication for security from punishments. When did you last sincerely implore Allah to protect you from the Fire with the same urgency as if it were right in front of you? If you haven't, what is stopping you from doing so right now?
?
The author commands you to 'reflect' on the Quran's general use of Hell's names. Have you ever read the threats of Hell in the Quran and comforted yourself by thinking 'that's for the Jews' or 'that's for the hypocrites'? How does this lesson shatter that false comfort? What specific change will you make in your daily recitation of the Quran?

The Fire of This World Originates from Jahannam
Ibn al-‘Arabi narrates the origin of worldly fire from Jahannam with details of its cooling and color changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tradition linking worldly fire to Hellfire and the symbolic process of its transformation.

Core Idea
Worldly fire is cooled Hellfire, tamed for human benefit.

This passage presents a tradition from Ibn al-‘Arabi explaining the origin of worldly fire. It asserts that the fire we use in daily life is derived from the fire of Jahannam, which was extinguished twice in the sea to make it usable. Moreover, the process of its cooling and color change over millennia is described, giving a profound link between the mundane and the eschatological.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the specific details: origin, extinguishing, and color changes.
Pay attention to the sequence of events.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the origin (Jahannam), the cooling process (twice in the sea), and the color transformation (three stages of a thousand years each).
3
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this tradition matters: it connects a familiar physical phenomenon to the unseen reality of Hell, emphasizing that all things ultimately trace back to the divine realm.
This helps in understanding the Islamic worldview.
4
Address common misunderstanding
Note the common misconception that worldly fire is unrelated to Hellfire. This tradition directly asserts a material connection, correcting that misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر ابن العربي: أنَّ) نار الدُّنيا من جهنّم طفئت في البحر مرَّتين، ولولا ذلك لم ينتفع بها، وبعد أخذ نار الدُّنيا منها أوقد عليها ألف سنة حتى ابيضت، ثمَّ ألف سنة حتَّى احمرَّت، ثمَّ ألف سنة حتّى اسودت فهي سوداء مظلمة).
And Ibn al-‘Arabi mentioned that the fire of this world is from Jahannam; it was extinguished in the sea twice. Were it not for that, it would not be benefited from. And after taking the fire of this world from it, it was ignited upon it for a thousand years until it became white, then a thousand years until it became red, then a thousand years until it became black; so it is black and dark.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage reports a tradition from Ibn al-‘Arabi (likely the Andalusian scholar) about the origin of worldly fire. It states that the fire used in this world comes from the Hellfire (Jahannam). This fire was extinguished in the sea twice. Without this cooling process, the fire would be too intense to be used beneficially. Additionally, after the fire was taken from Jahannam, it was kindled upon (or over?) for a thousand years until it became white, then another thousand years until it became red, and then a third thousand years until it became black. Consequently, worldly fire is described as black and dark. This tradition serves to explain the familiar properties of fire while linking it to the eschatological reality of Hell.

Key Terms
نار الدنيا
جهنّم
ابن العربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worldly fire is unrelated to Hellfire and has no connection to the afterlife.
Correction: This tradition asserts a direct material connection: worldly fire originates from Jahannam and was cooled to be beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-‘Arabi, how many times was the fire of this world extinguished in the sea?
A) Once
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) A thousand times

Key Takeaway
Worldly fire is cooled Hellfire, transformed through a process of extinguishing and prolonged kindling, linking the familiar to the eschatological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you light a match or turn on your stove, how does knowing that this flame is cooled Hellfire change your awareness of Allah's mercy? Will you pause today to thank Him for taming what could destroy you?
?
The fire endured three harsh transformations to become useful to you. What does this teach you about how Allah refines your own trials? What 'cooling' process do you need to submit to so that your struggles become a benefit to others?
?
If the smallest spark here comes from Hell, what does that imply about the reality of what awaits the heedless? How does this truth shake your complacency in sins you consider 'small'? What will you change today because of this?

The Levels of Paradise
Al-Sawi presents opinions on the number of Paradises and lists the seven names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on the number of Paradises and the names of its levels.

Core Idea
The highest Paradise is al-Firdaws, above which lies the Throne of the Merciful.

This passage begins the discussion of the Abode of Reward by presenting three views on how many Paradises there are. Ibn Abbas and a group list seven names, each a distinct level, while the majority hold that there are four based on Surat al-Rahman. A third view considers Paradise as one with multiple names. Let's explore these perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and locate the three views: seven, four, one. Notice the evidence cited for each.
The majority opinion uses Surat al-Rahman as proof.
2
List the seven names
From the first view, write down the seven names of Paradise in order from highest to lowest.
Al-Firdaws is the highest, then Jannat al-Ma'wa, etc.
3
Understand the unified view
Consider the argument that Paradise is one entity with multiple names. Reflect on why each name is appropriate.
This view avoids multiplying entities unnecessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دار الثَّواب وهي سبع أعلاها وأفضلها الفردوس، وفوقها عرسُ الرَّحمن، ومنها تنفجر أنهار الجنَّة، فجنَّةُ المأوى، فجنَّةُ الخُلد، فجنَّةُ النَّعيم، فجنَّةُ عَدْن، فدارُ السَّلام، فدار الجلال، هذا ما ذهب إليه ابن عباس وجماعة. وذهب الجمهور إلى أنَّها أربع بدليل ما في سورة (الرحمن)(١)، وقيل: الجنة واحدة، وما تقدَّم أسماء لمسمّى واحد، إذ كل اسم صالح لها . وقد نظم ذلك شيخنا الأمير بقوله : من الطويل] جهنَّم للعاصي لظى ليهودها وحطمة دار للنَّصارى أولى الصَّمم سعير عذاب الصَّابئين ودارهم مجوس لها سقر جحيم لذي صنم وهاوية دار النفاق وقيتها وأسأل ربَّ العرش أمناً من النّقم وما ذكره الشَّرح تبع فيه بعض الأحاديث، ولكنَّ آيات القرآن شاهدة بأنَّ كلَّ اسم من تلك الأسماء يطلق على ما يعم الجميع؛ لأنه يذكر صفات الكفَّار بأي وجه، ويعبر عن وعيدهم بأي اسم من هذه الأسماء، فتدبَّر . وذكر ابن العربي: أنَّ نار الدُّنيا من جهنّم طفئت في البحر مرَّتين، ولولا ذلك لم ينتفع بها، وبعد أخذ نار الدُّنيا منها أوقد عليها ألف سنة حتى ابيضت، ثمَّ ألف سنة حتَّى احمرَّت، ثمَّ ألف سنة حتّى اسودت فهي سوداء مظلمة). قوله : (دار الثَّواب) أي : ولها ثمانية أبواب كبار ؛ باب الشهادتين، وباب الصَّلاة، وباب الصيام، وباب الزَّكاة، وباب الحج، وباب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر، وباب الصّلة وباب الجهاد في سبيل الله، ومن داخلها عشرة أبواب صغار ومحلُّ الجنَّة فوق السَّماوات السَّبع ، ولم يصح في محلّ النَّار خبر.
Dar al-Thawab (Abode of Reward) is seven, the highest and best of which is al-Firdaws, and above it is the Throne of the Merciful, and from it gush forth the rivers of Paradise: then Jannat al-Ma'wa, then Jannat al-Khuld, then Jannat al-Na'im, then Jannat 'Adn, then Dar al-Salam, then Dar al-Jalal. This is what Ibn Abbas and a group held. And the majority held that they are four, as evidenced by what is in Surat al-Rahman (1), and it was said: Paradise is one, and what preceded are names for a single named entity, since each name is suitable for it. And our teacher al-Amir composed a poem on that, in the meter of al-Tawil: 'Jahannam for the disobedient, Laza for its Jews, and Hatama a house for the Christians, the deaf ones. Sa'ir is the punishment of the Sabians, and their house; the Magians have Saqar; Jahim for the idol worshipper; and Hawiya is the house of hypocrisy and its heat. And I ask the Lord of the Throne for safety from punishments.' And what the commentary mentioned, following some hadiths, but the verses of the Quran bear witness that every one of those names is applied to what is common to all; because He mentions the attributes of the disbelievers in any manner, and expresses their threat with any of these names, so reflect. And Ibn al-Arabi mentioned that the fire of this world is from Jahannam, extinguished in the sea twice, and were it not for that, no benefit would be had from it; and after taking the fire of this world from it, it was kindled upon it for a thousand years until it became white, then a thousand years until it became red, then a thousand years until it became black, so it is black and dark. His saying: (Dar al-Thawab) i.e., it has eight large gates: the gate of the two testimonies, the gate of prayer, the gate of fasting, the gate of zakat, the gate of Hajj, the gate of commanding good and forbidding evil, the gate of kinship ties, and the gate of jihad in the way of Allah. And inside it are ten small gates. And the location of Paradise is above the seven heavens, and no report has been authenticated regarding the location of Hell.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the names, levels, gates, and locations of Paradise and Hell. It begins by discussing the number of gardens (Paradises). Ibn Abbas and a group hold that there are seven, naming them in ascending order: Firdaws (the highest), the Throne of the Merciful (above Firdaws, from which rivers flow), then Jannat al-Ma'wa, al-Khuld, al-Na'im, 'Adn, Dar al-Salam, and Dar al-Jalal. The majority, however, say they are four, citing Surat al-Rahman (46, 62) which mentions two gardens for those who fear their Lord, and two others below them. Another opinion says Paradise is one with multiple names. The author then presents a poem by his teacher al-Amir that assigns specific names of Hell to different sinful groups: Jahannam for the general disobedient, Laza for Jews, Hatama for Christians, Sa'ir for Sabians, Saqar for Magians, Jahim for idol worshippers, and Hawiya for hypocrites. The commentary then clarifies that these names are not exclusive; Quranic evidence shows that any name can apply to all disbelievers collectively. The author adds a physical description from Ibn al-Arabi: the worldly fire originates from Hellfire, but was extinguished in the sea twice to make it usable; after its extraction it was heated for three thousand years, turning white, then red, then black. The passage then returns to the commentary on 'Dar al-Thawab', explaining that Paradise has eight large gates, each associated with a major act of worship: the two testimonies (shahada), prayer, fasting, zakat, Hajj, commanding good and forbidding evil, maintaining kinship ties, and jihad. Additionally, there are ten smaller gates inside. Finally, the location of Paradise is above the seven heavens, while no authentic report specifies the location of Hell.

Key Terms
فردوس
جنة المأوى
جنة الخلد
جنة النعيم
جنة عدن
دار السلام
دار الجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest level of Paradise?
A) Jannat 'Adn
B) al-Firdaws
C) Dar al-Salam
D) Jannat al-Ma'wa

Key Takeaway
There are three scholarly views on the number of Paradises, with the highest level being al-Firdaws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions that from al-Firdaws gush forth the rivers of Paradise. Is your daily life drawing you closer to that source, or are you drifting away to lesser delights? What one change can you make this week to align your heart more with the highest Paradise?
?
The majority opinion holds that there are four Paradises, while Ibn Abbas says seven. This difference does not change the truth that Paradise is real and awaits the believers. Does your certainty about the afterlife shape how you spend each hour? Or do you live as if this world is all there is?
?
The Throne of the Merciful is above al-Firdaws. Contemplate that the Creator is above His creation, yet His mercy encompasses all. Are you living with the awareness that you are always in His presence, and that your ultimate destination depends on your response to His guidance today?

The Names of Hell
Al-Sawi cites a poem on the names of Hell and clarifies their Quranic usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific names of Hell mentioned in the poem and the Quranic proof that these names apply to all disbelievers.

Core Idea
All names of Hell refer to one reality; no name is exclusive to one group.

Al-Sawi presents a poem by his teacher al-Amir that assigns each name of Hell to a specific sinful group. However, he immediately clarifies that the Quran uses these names interchangeably for all disbelievers. This lesson explores the names and the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem
Read the poem carefully and note the seven names of Hell and the group associated with each.
The poem uses meter al-Tawil.
2
Identify the Quranic correction
Find where Al-Sawi says the Quran uses all names for all disbelievers. Understand why this is important.
The commentary (sharh) is based on some hadiths, but the Quran is the final authority.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the implications: no sinner is confined to a specific part of Hell; all names describe the same punishment.
This prevents a literal reading of the poem.

The Gates of Paradise and Its Location
Al-Sawi describes the eight gates of Paradise and its location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn the eight great gates of Paradise and the ten small gates inside, as well as its location above the heavens.

Core Idea
Paradise has eight gates corresponding to major acts of worship, and it is located above the seven heavens.

After discussing the names of Paradise and Hell, Al-Sawi turns to the gates of Paradise. He lists eight large gates, each associated with a specific act of worship, and mentions ten smaller gates inside. He also clarifies that Paradise is above the seven heavens, while no authentic report locates Hell.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of eight gates
Read the passage and identify each of the eight large gates. Note the act of worship associated with each.
The gates are named after acts: shahada, salat, sawm, zakat, hajj, amr bil ma'ruf, sila, jihad.
2
Note the ten small gates
The passage mentions ten small gates inside Paradise. Consider what they might represent.
They are not named in this passage.
3
Understand the location
The passage states Paradise is above the seven heavens, and no authentic report specifies Hell's location.
This contrasts with some traditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the majority held that they are four, as evidenced by what is in Surat al-Rahman (1), and it was said: Paradise is one, and what preceded are names for a single named entity, since each name is suitable for it. And our teacher al-Amir composed a poem on that, in the meter of al-Tawil: 'Jahannam for the disobedient, Laza for its Jews, and Hatama a house for the Christians, the deaf ones. Sa'ir is the punishment of the Sabians, and their house; the Magians have Saqar; Jahim for the idol worshipper; and Hawiya is the house of hypocrisy and its heat. And I ask the Lord of the Throne for safety from punishments.' And what the commentary mentioned, following some hadiths, but the verses of the Quran bear witness that every one of those names is applied to what is common to all; because He mentions the attributes of the disbelievers in any manner, and expresses their threat with any of these names, so reflect. And Ibn al-Arabi mentioned that the fire of this world is from Jahannam, extinguished in the sea twice, and were it not for that, no benefit would be had from it; and after taking the fire of this world from it, it was kindled upon it for a thousand years until it became white, then a thousand years until it became red, then a thousand years until it became black, so it is black and dark. His saying: (Dar al-Thawab) i.e., it has eight large gates: the gate of the two testimonies, the gate of prayer, the gate of fasting, the gate of zakat, the gate of Hajj, the gate of commanding good and forbidding evil, the gate of kinship ties, and the gate of jihad in the way of Allah. And inside it are ten small gates. And the location of Paradise is above the seven heavens, and no report has been authenticated regarding the location of Hell.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the names, levels, gates, and locations of Paradise and Hell. It begins by discussing the number of gardens (Paradises). Ibn Abbas and a group hold that there are seven, naming them in ascending order: Firdaws (the highest), the Throne of the Merciful (above Firdaws, from which rivers flow), then Jannat al-Ma'wa, al-Khuld, al-Na'im, 'Adn, Dar al-Salam, and Dar al-Jalal. The majority, however, say they are four, citing Surat al-Rahman (46, 62) which mentions two gardens for those who fear their Lord, and two others below them. Another opinion says Paradise is one with multiple names. The author then presents a poem by his teacher al-Amir that assigns specific names of Hell to different sinful groups: Jahannam for the general disobedient, Laza for Jews, Hatama for Christians, Sa'ir for Sabians, Saqar for Magians, Jahim for idol worshippers, and Hawiya for hypocrites. The commentary then clarifies that these names are not exclusive; Quranic evidence shows that any name can apply to all disbelievers collectively. The author adds a physical description from Ibn al-Arabi: the worldly fire originates from Hellfire, but was extinguished in the sea twice to make it usable; after its extraction it was heated for three thousand years, turning white, then red, then black. The passage then returns to the commentary on 'Dar al-Thawab', explaining that Paradise has eight large gates, each associated with a major act of worship: the two testimonies (shahada), prayer, fasting, zakat, Hajj, commanding good and forbidding evil, maintaining kinship ties, and jihad. Additionally, there are ten smaller gates inside. Finally, the location of Paradise is above the seven heavens, while no authentic report specifies the location of Hell.

باب الزكاة

باب الجهاد

The Current Existence and Permanence of Paradise and Hell
Al-Sawi defends the current existence and eternal permanence of Paradise and Hell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that Paradise and Hell are already created and eternally enduring, and how key Quranic verses are interpreted to refute opposing views.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell are real, created now, and will never perish.

This passage from al-Sawi's gloss defends a core Sunni belief: that Paradise and Hell are not just future realities but already exist. He counters those who say they will only be created in the Hereafter, those who claim they will eventually perish, and philosophers who deny their physical reality. To do so, he offers specific interpretations of verses that might seem to suggest otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow and the groups being addressed.
Notice that the passage begins with a declaration and then systematically addresses three opposing groups.
2
Identify the three opposing groups
The passage refutes the Mu'tazila, the Jahmiyya, and the philosophers. For each, note the specific claim they make and how al-Sawi counters it.
Mu'tazila: Paradise and Hell not yet created. Jahmiyya: they will perish. Philosophers: denial of physical afterlife.
3
Understand the scriptural interpretations
Al-Sawi interprets two Quranic verses to support his position. Look at how he explains the phrases 'as long as the heavens and the earth endure' and 'except what your Lord wills' to maintain the eternity of Paradise and Hell.
The interpretation distinguishes between the heavens/earth of this world and those of the afterlife.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this issue matters. If Paradise and Hell are not eternal, divine justice and promise are compromised. This lesson grounds a key eschatological belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالجنة والنار والجنة والنار موجودتان الآن، والجنَّةُ هي التي أُهبط منها آدم عليه السَّلام، خلافاً للمعتزلة الذاهبين إلى أنّهما سيوجدان في الآخرة، وأنّ قوله : (موجودتان (الآن :أي: ويبقيان ببقاء الله تعالى، خلافاً للجهمية القائلين بفنائهما، وفناء أهلهما وهم كفَّار، وقوله تعالى : (مَا دَامَتِ السَّمَوَاتُ وَالْأَرْضُ ) (١) : المراد سقف الجنَّة والنار وأرضهما، لا سماء الدُّنيا وأرضهما؛ لتبدلهما قبل الدخول. وقوله تعالى : إِلَّا مَا شَاءَ رَبُّكَ ) (٢) ؛ أي: بدخول النَّارِ أَوَّلاً ، ثمَّ يخرجون منها، فخلودهم إما من غير سابقة عذاب، أو مع سابقته وهذا في السُّعداء، ويقال في الأشقياء : إلَّا ما شاء ربك من مدة البرزخ والموقف، وانظر بسط الأجوبة في حاشيتنا على الجلالين إن شئت. قوله : (إلى أنَّهما سيوجدان في الآخرة: أي: وخلافاً للفلاسفة؛
Belief in Paradise and Hell: Paradise and Hell exist now, and Paradise is that from which Adam, peace be upon him, was descended, contrary to the Mu'tazila who go to the view that they will exist in the Hereafter, and that... His saying: (Existing now), i.e., and they will remain by the lasting of Allah Almighty, contrary to the Jahmiyya who say that they will perish, and that their inhabitants will perish, and they are disbelievers. And His saying, exalted is He: (As long as the heavens and the earth endure) (1) means the ceiling of Paradise and Hell and their ground, not the heaven of this world and its ground; because they will be changed before entry. And His saying, exalted is He: (Except what your Lord wills) (2), i.e., by entering the Fire first, then they will exit from it. So their eternity is either without preceding punishment, or with its precedence, and this applies to the blessed. And it is said regarding the wretched: Except what your Lord wills of the period of the Barzakh and the Station. And see the detailed answers in our gloss on al-Jalalayn if you wish. His saying: (That they will exist in the Hereafter), i.e., and contrary to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument for the current existence and eternal permanence of Paradise and Hell. It begins with a statement of faith: Paradise and Hell exist now. Paradise is specifically the one from which Adam was descended. This is in opposition to the Mu'tazila, who believe that Paradise and Hell will only be created in the Hereafter. The commentary then explains the phrase 'existing now' (موجودتان الآن) to mean that they will also remain eternally by the lasting of Allah, contrary to the Jahmiyya who claim that both Paradise and Hell and their inhabitants (the disbelievers) will perish. To reconcile the verse about 'as long as the heavens and the earth endure' (Surah Hud, 107), the commentator specifies that the heavens and earth mentioned there refer to the ceiling and ground of Paradise and Hell, not the current heavens and earth, which will be replaced before entry. Furthermore, the verse 'except what your Lord wills' is interpreted as meaning the entry of the blessed into the Fire first (if they have sins) before exiting, so their eternity is either without prior punishment (for the pure) or with it (for sinners). For the wretched (disbelievers), the exception refers to the period of the Barzakh (intermediate state) and the Station (the Day of Judgment). The commentator then references his own extended answers in his gloss on Tafsir al-Jalalayn. Finally, the commentary on the phrase 'that they will exist in the Hereafter' states that the Mu'tazila are also opposed by the philosophers (who deny bodily resurrection or the existence of Paradise and Hell altogether).

المعتزلة
الجهمية
الفلاسفة
خلود
برزخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Paradise and Hell are not yet created and will only appear on the Day of Judgment.
Correction: The Sunni position, based on Quran and Sunnah, is that both are already created and exist now.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of the Jahmiyya regarding Paradise and Hell?
A) They do not exist yet
B) They will perish along with their inhabitants
C) They are eternal but not created
D) They are only spiritual realms

Key Takeaway
Paradise and Hell are already created and will never perish; apparent scriptural difficulties are resolved by careful interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Paradise and Hell are as real as the ground you walk on? What would change in your daily routine if you felt their reality every moment?
?
The Jahmiyya denied Hell's permanence because they could not reconcile it with Allah's mercy. How do you reconcile the reality of eternal punishment with your own understanding of Allah's attributes? Does it challenge you to take sin more seriously?
?
If you knew that the eternity of Paradise is already prepared and waiting, what worldly attachment would you struggle to let go of right now? What step will you take today to prioritize that eternal reality over temporary pleasure?

The Current and Eternal Existence of Paradise and Hell
Al-Sawi affirms Paradise and Hell exist now, refuting Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni belief that Paradise and Hell currently exist and will never perish, and the refutation of the Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya on this issue.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell exist now and will never end; all other views are condemned.

This passage from Al-Sawi affirms a key article of faith: the real, present existence of Paradise and Hell. It counters two groups who deny this—the Mu'tazila who postpone their existence and the Jahmiyya who teach their annihilation. The text also clarifies Quranic verses that might be misunderstood, and gives details about Paradise's location.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central affirmation
Read the opening lines where Al-Sawi states belief in Paradise and Hell existing now. This is the foundational claim.
Note that 'now' means from the moment of creation until eternity.
2
Identify the two refuted groups
Find where Al-Sawi mentions the Mu'tazila (who say Paradise and Hell will only exist in the Hereafter) and the Jahmiyya (who say they will perish). Understand their errors.
The Mu'tazila are refuted by noting Adam's descent from Paradise; the Jahmiyya are refuted by affirming eternal endurance.
3
Examine the scriptural interpretations
Look at how Al-Sawi interprets the verses 'as long as the heavens and the earth endure' and 'except what your Lord wills' to support the permanence of Paradise and Hell.
These verses are often misunderstood; Al-Sawi explains they refer to the specific heavens/earth of the afterlife.

Source Text

الإيمان بالجنة والنار والجنة والنار موجودتان الآن والجنة هي التي أهبط منها آدم عليه السلام خلافا للمعتزلة الذاهبين إلى أنهما سيوجدان في الآخرة وأن وباب الصلاة وباب الصيام وباب الزكاة وباب الحج وباب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر وباب الصلة وباب الجهاد في سبيل الله ومن داخلها عشرة أبواب صغار ومحل الجنة فوق السموات السبع ولم يصح في محل النار خبر. قوله: (موجودتان الآن) أي: ويبقيان ببقاء الله تعالى خلافا للجهمية القائلين بفنائهما وفناء أهليهما وهم كفار وقوله تعالى: (ما دامت السماوات والأرض) المراد سقف الجنة والنار وأرضهما لا سماء الدنيا وأرضهما لتبدلهما قبل الدخول وقوله تعالى: (إلا ما شاء ربك) أي: بدخول النار أولا ثم يخرجون منها فخلودهم إما من غير سابقة عذاب أو مع سابقته وهذا في السعداء ويقال في الأشقياء: إلا ما شاء ربك من مدة البرزخ والموقف وانظر بسط الأجوبة في حاشيتنا على الجلالين إن شئت. قوله: (إلى أنهما سيوجدان في الآخرة) أي: وخلافا للفلاسفة؛ وقوله تعالى: (ومن دونهما جنتان) جنة عدن وجنة الفردوس. آدم أهبط من بستان على ربوة من الأرض.
Belief in Paradise and Hell. Paradise and Hell exist now. Paradise is that from which Adam was descended, contrary to the Mu'tazila who go to that they will exist in the Hereafter, and that... And gate of prayer, gate of fasting, gate of zakat, gate of hajj, gate of commanding good and forbidding evil, gate of kinship, gate of jihad in the way of Allah. And inside it are ten small gates. And the place of Paradise is above the seven heavens, and no report has been authenticated regarding the place of Hell. His saying: (exist now) means: and they remain by the enduring of Allah Most High, contrary to the Jahmiyya who say they will perish and their inhabitants will perish, and they are disbelievers. And the saying of Allah Most High: (as long as the heavens and the earth endure) [Hud 107] means the ceiling of Paradise and Hell and their ground, not the heaven of the world and its earth, because they will be changed before entry. And the saying of Allah Most High: (except what your Lord wills) [Hud 107] means: by entering the Fire first, then they will come out of it. So their eternity is either without preceding punishment or with its precedence, and this is about the blessed. And it is said regarding the wretched: except what your Lord wills from the duration of the Barzakh and the Standing. And look at the detailed answers in our gloss on al-Jalalayn if you wish. His saying: (that they will exist in the Hereafter) means: and contrary to the philosophers; And the saying of Allah Most High: (And besides these two are two gardens) [Ar-Rahman 62] Garden of Eden and Garden of Firdaws. Adam was descended from a garden on a hill of the earth.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal affirmation that Paradise and Hell currently exist. It begins by stating belief in them, clarifying that Paradise is where Adam was from, opposing the Mu'tazila who say they will only exist in the Hereafter. It then lists the gates of Paradise and notes its location above the seven heavens, with no authentic location for Hell. The commentary explains 'exist now' as meaning they will remain forever by Allah's power, refuting the Jahmiyya who claim they will perish. The verse 'as long as the heavens and the earth endure' is interpreted as referring to the specific heavens and earth of Paradise and Hell, not the worldly ones, which will be replaced. The verse 'except what your Lord wills' is explained regarding the wicked entering Hell first then exiting (for sinful believers), while the blessed never enter. It also mentions the Barzakh and Standing for the wretched. The passage ends by refuting philosophers and noting that Adam was descended from a garden on earth.

Belief in Jinn and Angels: Their Nature and Obligation
Al-Sawi defines jinn and angels, states obligation to believe, and explains attributes with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic creed regarding jinn and angels, including their definitions, attributes, and the obligation to believe in them.

Core Idea
Jinn are fiery shapeshifters; angels are luminous and infallible; denying either is disbelief.

In this lesson, we delve into the sam'iyyat (matters known from revelation) regarding the unseen beings that share our world: the jinn and the angels. Al-Sawi provides clear definitions and scriptural evidence, establishing the necessity of belief. Understanding their nature helps prevent confusion and safeguards one's faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: first jinn, then angels, then the obligation to believe, followed by a Quranic verse and commentary.
Pay attention to the detailed attributes given for each type of being.
2
Identify key attributes of jinn and angels
List the essential characteristics: jinn are subtle fiery bodies with ability to take any form; angels are subtle spiritual luminous bodies with ability to take beautiful forms, and they are infallible.
The Quranic verse (At-Tahrim 6) supports the infallibility of angels.
3
Understand the consequence of denial
Note that denying the jinn is disbelief because it contradicts the Quran. Also note that some denied the existence of the angels Munkar and Nakir, but belief in them is required.
This shows that these beliefs are not optional but foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالملائكة والجن ملك، (و) يجب الإيمان بوجود (الجن) وهم: أجسام لطيفة نارية لهم قدرة على التشكلات، (و) بوجود (الأملاك) وعصمتهم أيضا، قال تعالى: لا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون، جمع ﴾ وهو: جسم لطيف روحاني نوراني له القدرة على التشكلات الجميلة. ويجب الإيمان بهم إجمالا فيمن علم منهم إجمالا، وتفصيلا فيمن علم منهم تفصيلا بالشخص؛ كجبريل وإسرافيل وميكائيل وعزرائيل، وهم رؤساء الملائكة عليهم الصلاة والسلام أجمعين، ومنكر ونكير فإنهم أنكروا وجودهما بالمرة. قوله: (ويجب الإيمان بوجود الجن) أي: ومن أنكر وجودهم كفر لمصادمة القرآن. قوله: (على التشكلات) أي: بأي صورة جميلة أو قبيحة، وتحكم عليهم الصورة. قوله: (على التشكلات الجميلة) المراد بها ما عدا الخسيسة؛ كالكلب والخنزير فيشمل الفظيعة الهائلة؛ كمالك خازن النار، ومنكر ونكير، وعزرائيل في إتيانهم الكفار، ولا تحكم عليهم الصورة.
Belief in the angels and the jinn. An angel, and it is obligatory to believe in the existence of the jinn, and they are: subtle bodies, fiery, having ability to take forms, and to believe in the existence of the angels and their infallibility as well. He said Most High: (They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded) [At-Tahrim 6]. Their plural is: a subtle spiritual luminous body having ability to take beautiful forms. And it is obligatory to believe in them in general regarding those known in general, and in detail regarding those known in detail by person, such as Jibril, Israfil, Mika'il, and 'Azra'il, and they are the chiefs of the angels, peace be upon them all, and Munkar and Nakir, for they denied their existence entirely. His saying: (And it is obligatory to believe in the existence of the jinn) means: and whoever denies their existence disbelieves, because it contradicts the Qur'an. His saying: (on the taking of forms) means: in any form, beautiful or ugly, and the form overpowers them. His saying: (on the beautiful forms) means: by that which is other than lowly forms, like the dog and the pig, so it includes the terrifying and huge forms, like Malik the keeper of Hell, and Munkar and Nakir, and 'Azra'il when they come to the disbelievers, and the form does not overpower them.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage covers the obligation to believe in jinn and angels. It defines jinn as subtle fiery bodies with the ability to shapeshift into various forms. Angels are defined as subtle spiritual luminous bodies capable of taking beautiful forms, and they are infallible, supported by a Quranic verse. Belief is obligatory: general for those known generally, and specific for named angels like Jibril, Israfil, Mika'il, and 'Azra'il, who are the chiefs, as well as Munkar and Nakir (some denied the latter two). The commentary clarifies that denying jinn leads to disbelief. It explains that jinn's shapeshifting ability applies to any form, beautiful or ugly, and the form overpowers them. For angels, the beautiful forms exclude lowly forms like dogs and pigs but include terrifying ones like Malik, Munkar, Nakir, and 'Azra'il when visiting disbelievers; for angels, the form does not overpower them (they retain their essence).

Key Terms
جن
ملك
تشكلات
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that jinn and angels are purely spiritual beings without any physical form.
Correction: In reality, jinn are made of fire and have subtle bodies; angels are made of light and have luminous bodies. Both can take on forms, though angels only take beautiful forms and are not overpowered by them.

Checkpoint

A) Angels are fiery bodies; denying jinn is disbelief.
B) Angels are luminous spiritual bodies; denying jinn is disbelief.
C) Angels are fiery bodies; denying jinn is permissible.
D) Angels are spiritual bodies without any physical form; denying jinn is disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Belief in jinn (fiery shapeshifters) and angels (luminous infallible beings) is obligatory upon every Muslim, with denial constituting disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in jinn and angels intellectually, but when you are alone at night and hear a strange noise, does your heart truly trust that Allah is the only protector? Are you still aware that jinn are present and can take forms, yet you forget that they too are under Allah's command? How can you deepen your consciousness of the unseen while remaining firm in tawheed? Commit today to recite the morning and evening adhkar with full presence of heart.
?
Angels are described as never disobeying Allah. They are infallible—something you are not. When you sin, do you feel the weight of your disobedience compared to their perfect obedience? Does that motivate you to repent immediately? Set a specific sin you struggle with and resolve today to abandon it completely for the sake of Allah, seeking His forgiveness.
?
If denying the existence of jinn is disbelief (kufr), have you ever harbored doubts about their reality because you cannot see them? What does that say about your trust in the Quran’s revelation? How can you strengthen your conviction in the unseen? Make a sincere dua tonight asking Allah to increase your certainty in all that He has revealed.

Definition and Obligation of Belief in Jinn
Defines jinn and establishes obligation to believe in them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic definition of jinn, their nature as subtle fiery beings capable of taking forms, and why belief in them is obligatory.

Core Idea
Jinn are real, subtle fiery beings with the power to change form; denying them is disbelief.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary defines the jinn and establishes the doctrinal ruling regarding belief in them. It not only affirms their existence but also describes their nature as subtle fiery bodies capable of assuming various forms. Understanding this definition is crucial for a correct Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the two parts: the original text and the commentator's gloss.
Notice that the commentary uses 'qawlu' to indicate explanation of specific phrases.
2
Identify the obligation and its consequence
The passage clearly states that belief in jinn is obligatory. What does the commentator say about someone who denies them? That is a key doctrinal point.
Denial leads to kufr because it contradicts the Quran.
3
Understand the definition of jinn
What are jinn made of? What special ability do they have? The passage gives two characteristics: they are subtle bodies, fiery, and have the ability to take on forms.
The term 'lāṭifa' indicates they are not dense like human bodies.
4
Reflect on the significance of form-taking
The commentator explains that jinn can appear in any form, beautiful or ugly, and that they are dominated by the form. Think about what this implies for their nature and interaction with the physical world.
This helps counter misconceptions that jinn are purely spiritual.

Commentary
This passage defines the jinn and establishes the obligation to believe in them. It begins by stating the obligation of belief in their existence, then describes their nature as subtle (lāṭifa) bodies, fiery in origin, capable of assuming various forms (tashakkulāt). The commentator (al-Sawi) explains the base text: the obligation is such that anyone who denies the existence of jinn commits disbelief (kufr) because such denial contradicts the explicit statements of the Qur'an. He further clarifies the phrase 'upon taking on forms' by noting that jinn can appear in any image, whether beautiful or ugly, and that they are controlled by the form they assume (i.e., they are subject to it). Thus the passage covers both the doctrinal ruling and a key aspect of jinn's nature.
