Commentary
This passage is a descriptive commentary on two categories of Paradise's inhabitants: the hur al-ayn (the women of Paradise) and the wildan (servant boys). It begins by stating that the women of Paradise are described as having wide eyes. Then it mentions the wildan: they are servant boys in the form of the servant boys of this world, and they are presented as gifts to the people of Paradise. There is a view that they are the children of disbelievers who die before puberty, but it is also reported that they are the servants of the people of Paradise. The commentary then expands on the hur al-ayn: it narrates a tradition that a cloud rained from the Throne, and from drops of mercy the hur were created. Each hur has a tent on the riverbank, forty miles wide, without a door. When a saint of Allah enters Paradise, the tent splits open at a door to show that no creature—angel or servant—has seen her; she is completely confined from the eyes of creatures. This illustrates the verse 'Hur, confined in pavilions.' The passage then declares that the correct view is that the women of this world are superior to the hur al-ayn by seventy thousand times. Finally, it returns to the wildan: they are called 'wildan' because they are in the shape and form of children. They are created directly in Paradise, like the hur, not from the children of this world; this is the correct opinion among several. An alternative view holds they are the children of believers who died young.

Belief in the Questioning of the Grave
Author explains the reality of Munkar and Nakir and the correct belief about the questioning in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic belief in the questioning of the grave by Munkar and Nakir, including their description, purpose, and the correct view that it applies to all deceased.

Core Idea
Munkar and Nakir question every deceased person — believer, unbeliever, and hypocrite.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary explains a key pillar of the samiyyat (matters heard from revelation): the questioning in the grave. The author clarifies that belief in this event is obligatory, but denial does not necessarily render one a disbeliever due to scholarly differences. He also provides a vivid description of the two angels and confirms that they question all deceased, contrary to a minority opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the English translation to get an overview of the content.
Notice the author's style: he begins with a ruling, then describes the angels, then addresses a disagreement.
2
Understand the obligation and ruling on deniers
The author states that belief in the questioning is obligatory, but its denier is not a kafir due to legitimate disagreement. Reflect on why this nuance matters in Islamic theology.
This reflects the principle that matters of samiyyat may have differing interpretations.
3
Examine the description of Munkar and Nakir
Note the etymology of their names and the physical description: black, blue-eyed, with eyes like copper cauldrons. Understand that these details come from revelation.
The names indicate the deceased's lack of recognition.
4
Identify the purpose of the angels
The passage explains that they honor the believer, expose the hypocrite, and frighten the unbeliever. Consider how this aligns with divine justice.
5
Determine who is questioned
The author affirms the correct view that all deceased are questioned, rejecting the minority opinion that only sinners are. Find the Arabic phrase that states this.
The phrase 'مؤمناً كان أو كافراً' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكسؤال الملكين) أي فهو ممَّا يجب الإيمان به، لكنَّ منكره لا يكفر للاختلاف فيه
منكر ونكير، وهما ملكان أسودان أزرقان؛ أي: أعينهما يأتيان للميت، مؤمناً كان أو كافراً أو منافقاً، بعد تمام الدفن في القبر الذي يستقرُّ فيه دائماً ، وعند انصراف النَّاس فيُقْعِدانه، .... لا يكفر للاختلاف فيه
قوله : (منكر) : بفتح الكاف اسم مفعول، ويجوز كسرها على فاعل ؛ لأنه ينكر على غيره كلامه . أنَّه اسم
قوله : ( ونكير) فعيل بمعنى مفعول ؛ من نكرت الرّجل إذا لم تعرفه، سمِّيا بذلك ؛ لأنَّ الميّت لم يكن يعرفهما ، ولم ير صورة مثل صورتهما . ا قوله : (أزرقان) : أي : أعينهما ؛ أي كقدور النُّحاس من شدة حمرتهما ، : يراها الناظر كالبرق الخاطف جعلهما الله تكرمة للمؤمن ليثبته وينصره، وهتكاً لستر المنافق في البرزخ، وإخافة للكافر ليتحيَّر في الجواب، وهما للمؤمن الطَّائع وغيره على الصحيح. وقيل : هما للكافر والعاصي، وأمَّا المؤمن الموفّق؛ فله ملكان آخران اسمهما مبشِّر وبشير . قوله: (مؤمناً كان أو كافراً ... إلخ): هذا هو الصحيح، خلافاً لقول ابن عبد البرّ والسُّيوطي، لا يسئل الكافر (۱)
His saying: ('and such as the questioning of the two angels'): meaning that it is among the things that must be believed in, but its denier is not a kafir due to disagreement about it. Munkar and Nakir, and they are two black, blue-eyed angels; meaning their eyes — they come to the deceased, whether believer, unbeliever, or hypocrite, after complete burial in the grave in which he permanently settles, and when the people depart, they sit him up... he is not a kafir due to disagreement about it. His saying: ('Munkar'): with fatha on the kaf it is a passive participle, and it is permissible with kasra as an active participle, because he denies the speech of others. That he is a name. His saying: ('and Nakir') is fa'il in the sense of maf'ul; from 'nakartu ar-rajul' if you did not recognize him; they were named thus because the deceased did not recognize them and had not seen a form like their form. His saying: ('blue-eyed'): meaning their eyes; meaning like copper cauldrons from the intensity of their redness; the beholder sees them like lightning. Allah made them an honor for the believer to stabilize and support him, and a tearing of the veil of the hypocrite in the barzakh, and a fright for the unbeliever so that he becomes confused in the answer. And they are for the obedient believer and others according to the correct view. And it was said: they are for the unbeliever and the disobedient; but as for the successful believer, he has two other angels named Mubashshir and Bashir. His saying: ('whether believer or unbeliever... etc.'): this is the correct view, contrary to the statement of Ibn Abd al-Barr and al-Suyuti that the unbeliever is not questioned.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage explains the belief in the questioning of the grave by the two angels Munkar and Nakir. It begins by stating that it is obligatory to believe in it, but its denier is not a kafir due to legitimate disagreement among scholars. Then it describes the angels: their names are Munkar (from 'inkar', denial) and Nakir (from 'nakara', not recognizing) because the deceased does not recognize them. They are black with blue eyes, their eyes like copper cauldrons from redness, looking like lightning. Their purpose is to honor the believer by strengthening him, to expose the hypocrite, and to frighten the unbeliever so he becomes confused in answering. According to the correct view, they come to every deceased person — believer, unbeliever, or hypocrite — though a minority view holds they come only to unbelievers and sinners, with successful believers having two other angels named Mubashshir and Bashir. The author confirms the first view as correct, rejecting the opinion of Ibn Abd al-Barr and al-Suyuti who said the unbeliever is not questioned.

Key Terms
سؤال القبر
منكر
نكير
مبشّر
بشير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The questioning in the grave only happens to believers, not to unbelievers or hypocrites.
Correction: The author confirms that the angels Munkar and Nakir question all deceased—believers, unbelievers, and hypocrites—and this is the correct view.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is true about the angels Munkar and Nakir?
A) They only question believers
B) They question all deceased except hypocrites
C) They question all deceased including believers, unbelievers, and hypocrites
D) They only question unbelievers

Key Takeaway
The angels Munkar and Nakir question every deceased person — believer, unbeliever, and hypocrite — after burial, as a test and honor for believers and a fright for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine lying in your grave and seeing Munkar and Nakir, what feeling does that stir in you? What can you do today to turn that fear into preparation?
?
The angels appear terrifying, yet for the believer they bring honor and stability. Do you live in a way that would make you a candidate for that honor, or are you relying on something else? What needs to change?
?
If you knew for certain you would be questioned tonight, what is the one doubt about your faith that you would most fear being exposed? How will you address that doubt before it is too late?

Munkar and Nakir: Angels of the Grave
Al-Sawi explains the names, appearance, and purpose of Munkar and Nakir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identities, appearance, and roles of the angels Munkar and Nakir, and why they are named as such.

Core Idea
Munkar and Nakir: unrecognized and awe-inspiring interrogators.

In this lesson, Al-Sawi introduces two angels central to the questioning in the grave: Munkar and Nakir. He explains how their names derive from the deceased not recognizing them, describes their striking appearance, and clarifies their distinct purposes for believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers. The lesson also touches on an alternate opinion that successful believers are visited by Mubashshir and Bashir instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the overall description of Munkar and Nakir settle in your mind.
2
Analyze the names
Focus on the linguistic derivations of 'Munkar' and 'Nakir'. Notice the possible meanings (active vs. passive participle) and why the names were chosen.
The key reason: the deceased does not recognize them.
3
Understand the appearance
Read the description of them as 'azraqān' – their eyes like copper cauldrons, flashing like lightning. Visualize the awe-inspiring form.
This description serves different purposes for different people.
4
Reflect on the purposes
Identify the three purposes: honor for the believer, exposure for the hypocrite, intimidation for the disbeliever. Also note the alternate opinion about Mubashshir and Bashir.
The correct view is that Munkar and Nakir come to all, but the successful believer may have other angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم سؤال القبر منكر ونكير، وهما ملكان أسودان أزرقان؛ أي: أعينهما يأتيان للميت، مؤمناً كان أو كافراً أو منافقاً، بعد تمام الدفن في القبر الذي يستقرُّ فيه دائماً، وعند انصراف النَّاس فيُقْعِدانه. قوله : (منكر) : بفتح الكاف اسم مفعول، ويجوز كسرها على فاعل ؛ لأنه ينكر على غيره كلامه . قوله : ( ونكير) فعيل بمعنى مفعول ؛ من نكرت الرّجل إذا لم تعرفه، سمِّيا بذلك ؛ لأنَّ الميّت لم يكن يعرفهما ، ولم ير صورة مثل صورتهما . قوله : (أزرقان) : أي : أعينهما ؛ أي كقدور النُّحاس من شدة حمرتهما ، يراها الناظر كالبرق الخاطف جعلهما الله تكرمة للمؤمن ليثبته وينصره، وهتكاً لستر المنافق في البرزخ، وإخافة للكافر ليتحيَّر في الجواب، وهما للمؤمن الطَّائع وغيره على الصحيح. وقيل : هما للكافر والعاصي، وأمَّا المؤمن الموفّق؛ فله ملكان آخران اسمهما مبشِّر وبشير .
Ruling of the questioning of the grave: Munkar and Nakir, and they are two black angels, bluish (i.e., their eyes). They come to the deceased, whether believer, disbeliever, or hypocrite, after the completion of burial in the grave in which he will permanently settle, and when the people depart, they sit him up. His saying: (Munkar): with fatḥa on the kāf, it is a passive participle, and it is permissible to break it (kasra) as an active participle, because he denies the speech of others. His saying: (Nakir): it is faʿīl in the sense of mafʿūl; from nakartu al-rajul if you did not know him. They are named thus because the deceased did not know them and did not see a form like their form. His saying: (azraqān): i.e., their eyes, like copper cauldrons due to the intensity of their redness, the onlooker sees them like a flash of lightning. Allah made them as an honor for the believer to establish him and give him victory, and as a stripping of the hypocrite's cover in the barzakh, and as an intimidation for the disbeliever so that he becomes confused in the answer. And they are for the obedient believer and others according to the correct view. And it was said: they are for the disbeliever and the sinner, but as for the successful believer, he has two other angels named Mubashshir and Bashīr.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines and describes the two angels Munkar and Nakir who conduct the questioning in the grave. It explains their names linguistically, their appearance (black, bluish eyes), and the purpose of their visitation. The author first states the ruling that questioning in the grave occurs, then names the angels. He notes that they come to every deceased person regardless of faith. Then he comments on the word 'Munkar' and 'Nakir', showing the linguistic derivation and why they are named so: because the deceased does not recognize them. Then he explains 'azraqān' meaning their eyes are extremely red like copper cauldrons, appearing like a flash of lightning. Then he enumerates the wisdom behind their coming: honor for the believer, exposure for the hypocrite, and intimidation for the disbeliever. Finally, he mentions the correct view that they come to both the obedient believer and others, and a different opinion that they are only for the disbeliever and sinner, while the successful believer has two other angels named Mubashshir and Bashir.

Key Terms
منكر ونكير
أزرقان
برزخ
مبشِّر وبشير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the angels are kind and gentle to everyone.
Correction: Their appearance is terrifying, and their purpose varies: they honor believers but intimidate disbelievers and expose hypocrites.

Checkpoint
Why are the two angels named Munkar and Nakir?
A) Because they are extremely red and flash like lightning.
B) Because the deceased does not recognize them and has not seen their form before.
C) Because they deny the speech of others.
D) Because they are black and have blue eyes.

Key Takeaway
Munkar and Nakir are named for being unrecognized, their appearance is terrifying, and their purpose varies by the deceased's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will meet Munkar and Nakir alone in your grave. What will you say when they ask, 'Who is your Lord, what is your religion, who is your prophet?' Are you living today as if you already know the answer, or are you avoiding the question?
?
The angels' eyes are like lightning — they see through every excuse. What part of your life, if exposed in the barzakh, would cause you shame right now? What changes can you make today so that their gaze brings you honor instead?
?
If the believer is strengthened and the hypocrite's cover is stripped, where do you stand? Are you banking on mere outward piety, or is your faith deep enough to stead you when you see what you've never seen before?
